Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate
10 min
The Dialectical Proof: Definition, Premises, and Application
Author defines dialectical proof and provides examples of well-known premises from Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical proof, distinguish its two types of premises (mashhurat and musallamat), and explain how Shari'a evidence can transform near-certain premises into absolute certainties.

Core Idea
Dialectical proofs use widely accepted premises to persuade, but divine revelation can elevate them to certainty.

In this passage, the author introduces the dialectical proof (al-hujja al-jadaliyya), a key tool in Islamic debate and argumentation. Unlike apodeictic proofs that rely on necessary premises, dialectical proofs draw on premises that are well-known to the masses or conceded by the opponent. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in kalam, fiqh, and other Islamic sciences, where persuasion often begins from commonly accepted truths.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition
Locate the sentence that defines dialectical proof in the Arabic text: 'هي الحجة المؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة...'. This is the core of the lesson.
2
Decompose the definition
Identify the genus (حجة – proof) and differentia (مؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة أو مسلم بها – composed of well-known or conceded premises). This tells you what makes a proof dialectical.
3
Classify the types of premises
Notice the two kinds: mashhurat (well-known to the masses, near-certain) and musallamat (conceded by the opponent, not necessarily certain).
4
Apply to Ghazali's examples
Take one of the author's examples, e.g., 'حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام' (we judge spreading peace as good). Explain why this is a mashhura premise.
5
Evaluate the Shari'a qualification
Understand that when such premises are supported by definitive revealed texts, they rise to absolute certainty (يقين جازم) for believers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الجدلية:
هي الحجة المؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة، تعتقد الجماهير مضمونها اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، فلا يشعر الذهن لأول النظر بأن نقيضه ممكن، أو المؤلفة من مقدمات يُسلّم بها المخاطب، ولكن هذه المقدمات لا ترقى في حقيقة حالها إلى مرتبة اليقين التام.
وتُقدَّم هذه الحجة الجدلية في أي طريق من طرق الاستدلال المباشر أو غير المباشر؛ «في التقابل - في العكس - في شكل منتج من أشكال القياس الصحيح - في الاستقراء - في التمثيل».
وتوجد أمثلتها في كل مجالات الفكر، وتكثر في قضايا الحق والواجب، كالقانونيات، والأخلاقيات، وتكثر أيضاً في الاجتماعيات، والسياسيات، والقضائيات، ومجالات التربية وغير ذلك.
فمن القضايا المشهورة التي تعتقدها الجماهير اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، الأمثلة التالية التي أوردها الإمام الغزالي:
حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام، وإطعام الطعام، وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والأحكام.
وحكمنا بقبح إيذاء الإنسان، وقتل الحيوان، ووضع البهتان، ورضاء الأزواج بفجور النسوان، ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران والطغيان.
ولكن هذا إذا لم نضع في اعتبارنا تعاليم الشرائع الربانية وما ثبت فيها بيقين، أما إذا وضعنا ذلك في اعتبارنا، فإن كثيراً من القضايا المشهورة ترتقي ببيانات الشريعة القاطعة إلى مرتبة اليقين الجازم، فتكون لدى المؤمنين بالشريعة - العارفين لما ثبت فيها بيقين - يقينيات.
The dialectical proof (al-hujja al-jadaliyya):
It is the proof composed of well-known premises (mashhurat), whose content the masses believe with a belief close to certainty, so the mind at first glance does not feel that its opposite is possible; or composed of premises that are conceded (musallam) by the interlocutor, but these premises do not, in reality, rise to the level of complete certainty.
And this dialectical proof is presented in any path of direct or indirect inference: in opposition (taqabul), in inversion ('aks), in a productive form of the correct syllogism, in induction (istiqra'), in analogy (tamthil).
And its examples exist in all fields of thought, and are abundant in matters of truth and duty, such as legal and ethical matters, and also abundant in social, political, judicial, and educational fields, and others.
Among the well-known premises that the masses believe with near-certainty are the following examples given by Imam al-Ghazali:
We judge as good the spreading of peace (salam), feeding food, maintaining family ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, and observing justice in cases and judgments.
And we judge as evil harming a person, killing an animal, slandering, husbands being pleased with the immorality of women, and meeting blessings with ingratitude and transgression.
But this is if we do not take into account the teachings of the divine laws and what is established therein with certainty. However, if we take that into account, then many of the well-known premises rise, through the definitive statements of the Shari'a, to the level of absolute certainty, thus becoming certainties for the believers in the Shari'a – those who know what is established therein with certainty.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines the dialectical proof (al-hujja al-jadaliyya), also known as jadal. It begins by describing two types of premises from which this proof is composed: (1) well-known premises (mashhurat) that the masses believe with near-certainty, such that the mind does not readily conceive of their opposite, and (2) premises conceded (musallam) by the opponent, though they may not be fully certain. The dialectical proof can be presented through any inferential path: direct or indirect, including opposition, inversion, valid syllogistic forms, induction, and analogy. The author notes that such proofs are common in fields like law, ethics, social sciences, politics, and education. The passage then provides examples from Imam al-Ghazali of well-known premises that are near-certain for the masses: judgments about the goodness of spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining family ties, truthfulness, and justice; and judgments about the evil of harming people, killing animals, slandering, marital immorality, and ingratitude. Finally, the author adds a crucial qualification: when considered in light of the definitive teachings of divine revelation (Shari'a), many of these well-known premises rise to the level of absolute certainty (yaqin jazim) for believers who know what is established in revelation. This demonstrates that dialectical premises can become certain within a religious framework.

Key Terms
الحجة الجدلية
المقدمات المشهورة
المقدمات المسلمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to well-known premises (mashhurat) when considered in light of definitive Shari'a teachings?
A) They remain near-certain
B) They become absolutely certain for believers
C) They become doubtful
D) They are rejected

Key Takeaway
Dialectical proofs rely on well-known or conceded premises, but when supported by definitive revelation, they yield certainty for believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following argument: 'Riba is forbidden because it is unjust. Injustice is condemned by the Shari'a.' Identify which type of premises (mashhura or musallama) each statement is, and explain whether the argument qualifies as a dialectical proof based on the passage.
?
Using the examples from Ghazali, pick one well-known premise (e.g., 'feeding food is good') and explain how it could be elevated to certainty by a clear Shari'a text. Provide an example of such a text (you may use a well-known hadith).

Dialectic -- Jadal
intermediate

Definition of Dialectical Argument and Mashhura Propositions
Defines dialectical argument and illustrates mashhura premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish dialectical arguments from demonstrative ones by identifying their epistemic status and types of premises.

Core Idea
Dialectical arguments rely on widely accepted or conceded premises, not absolute certainty.

In this lesson, we examine the definition of dialectical argument. You will learn how the author distinguishes it from demonstrative argument and why it remains valuable despite falling short of yaqin. The examples from Imam al-Ghazali illustrate what 'widely accepted' (mashhura) means in practice.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definiendum and the two defining conditions
Find the term being defined (حجة جدلية) and the two alternative criteria: premises that the masses believe with near-certainty, or premises conceded by the interlocutor. Notice the phrase 'أو' indicates two species.
The first type (mashhura) is closer to certainty than the second.
2
Analyze the epistemic level of mashhura
The author says these premises are believed with 'اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين' – belief approximating certainty, such that the mind does not immediately entertain the opposite. Contrast this with absolute yaqin.
This near-certainty is why dialectical arguments are persuasive.
3
Identify the al-Ghazali examples as mashhura
Read the examples: حسن إفشاء السلام, قبح إيذاء الإنسان, etc. They are moral judgments that people generally accept as obvious. Note that these are not necessarily certain unless supported by Sharia.
The author later adds that Sharia can elevate them to yaqin for believers.
4
Assess the completeness of the definition
Does the definition cover all dialectical arguments? It specifies the premise type but not the form. Later the author will list the forms (syllogism, analogy, etc.). This definition focuses on content.
A full understanding requires combining this with the next passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الجدلية:
هي الحجة المؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة، تعتقد الجماهير مضمونها اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، فلا يشعر الذهن لأول النظر بأن نقيضه ممكن، أو المؤلفة من مقدمات يُسلّم بها المخاطب، ولكن هذه المقدمات لا ترقى في حقيقة حالها إلى مرتبة اليقين التام.
وتُقدَّم هذه الحجة الجدلية في أي طريق من طرق الاستدلال المباشر أو غير المباشر؛ «في التقابل - في العكس - في شكل منتج من أشكال القياس الصحيح - في الاستقراء - في التمثيل».
وتوجد أمثلتها في كل مجالات الفكر، وتكثر في قضايا الحق والواجب، كالقانونيات، والأخلاقيات، وتكثر أيضاً في الاجتماعيات، والسياسيات، والقضائيات، ومجالات التربية وغير ذلك.
فمن القضايا المشهورة التي تعتقدها الجماهير اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، الأمثلة التالية التي أوردها الإمام الغزالي:
حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام، وإطعام الطعام، وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والأحكام.
وحكمنا بقبح إيذاء الإنسان، وقتل الحيوان، ووضع البهتان، ورضاء الأزواج بفجور النسوان، ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران والطغيان.
ولكن هذا إذا لم نضع في اعتبارنا تعاليم الشرائع الربانية وما ثبت فيها بيقين، أما إذا وضعنا ذلك في اعتبارنا، فإن كثيراً من القضايا المشهورة ترتقي ببيانات الشريعة القاطعة إلى مرتبة اليقين الجازم، فتكون لدى المؤمنين بالشريعة - العارفين لما ثبت فيها بيقين - يقينيات.
ومن أمثلة الحجج الجدلية في القرآن، الاستدلال على ضرورة اليوم الآخر بصفة العدل التي يتصف بها الخالق جلّ وعلا، وأن من مقتضى العدل عدم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، بين الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات والمفسدين في الأرض، بين المتقين والفجار.
قال الله تعالى في سورة (القلم 68):
﴿أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ (35) مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ (36)﴾.
وقال تعالى في سورة (ص 38):
﴿أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ (28)﴾.
وقد جاء هذا رداً على الذين أنكروا اليوم الآخر.
ويمكن أن نصوغ هذا الدليل على الوجه التالي:
لو لم يكن يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء حقيقة ثابتة، لكان واقع هذه الحياة يستلزم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، وهذه التسوية تستلزم نفي صفة العدل عن الخالق جلّ وعلا.
لكن صفة العدل لله ثابتة ∴ فلا تسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين ∴ فلابد من يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء ∴ فهذا اليوم الآخر حقيقة ثابتة
The dialectical argument: It is the argument composed of widely accepted premises, the masses believe their content with belief approximating certainty, such that the mind does not initially feel that its opposite is possible, or composed of premises conceded by the interlocutor, but these premises do not in reality rise to the level of complete certainty. And this dialectical argument is presented in any path of direct or indirect inference: in opposition (contraposition), in inversion, in a productive figure from the valid figures of syllogism, in induction, in analogy. Its examples are found in all fields of thought, and are abundant in issues of truth and duty, such as legal and ethical matters, and also in social, political, judicial, educational fields, and others. Among the widely accepted premises that the masses believe with belief approximating certainty are the following examples cited by Imam al-Ghazali: Our judgment of the goodness of spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, and observing justice in judgments and rulings. And our judgment of the ugliness of harming people, killing animals, slandering, husbands being pleased with their wives' immorality, and responding to blessings with ingratitude and transgression. But this is if we do not take into consideration the teachings of divine laws and what has been established therein with certainty; if we take that into consideration, many of the widely accepted premises rise, by the decisive evidences of the Sharia, to the level of absolute certainty, and thus become certainties for the believers in the Sharia who know what is established therein with certainty. And among the examples of dialectical arguments in the Quran is the inference of the necessity of the Last Day from the attribute of justice with which the Creator is characterized, and that justice requires not treating Muslims and criminals equally, not treating those who believe and do righteous deeds like those who spread corruption on earth, nor the righteous like the wicked. Allah said in Surah al-Qalam (68): ['Afa-nal Muslimina kal-mujrimun? Ma lakum kayfa tahkumun?'] And He said in Surah Sad (38): ['Am naj'alu alladhina amanu wa amilu al-salihati kal-mufsideen fi al-ardi am naj'alu al-muttaqeen kal-fujjar?'] And this came in response to those who denied the Last Day. And we can formulate this proof as follows: If there were no Last Day for reckoning and recompense as a fixed reality, then the reality of this life would necessitate equality between Muslims and criminals, and this equality would necessitate negating the attribute of justice from the Creator. But the attribute of justice for Allah is established. Therefore, there is no equality between Muslims and criminals. Therefore, there must be a Last Day for reckoning and recompense. Therefore, this Last Day is a fixed reality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the dialectical argument (hujja jadaliyya). It begins by explaining that dialectical arguments are composed of widely accepted premises (mashhura) that the public believes with near-certainty, such that the mind does not immediately conceive of their opposite. Alternatively, they may be composed of premises conceded by the interlocutor, though these premises do not reach the level of complete certainty. The author then lists the various logical forms dialectical arguments can take: contraposition, conversion, valid syllogistic figures, induction, and analogy. Examples from al-Ghazali are given: judgments about the goodness of social virtues and the ugliness of vices. These are widely accepted but not necessarily certain from a religious perspective unless supported by Sharia. The author then provides a Quranic example: arguing for the Last Day based on God's justice. The argument is formalized as a conditional syllogism: if there were no Last Day, then justice would be violated; but God is just; therefore, there must be a Last Day.

Key Terms
حجة جدلية
مشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the defining characteristic of a dialectical argument?
A) It uses only empirical premises.
B) It is composed of widely accepted or conceded premises that fall short of complete certainty.
C) It is always presented in the form of a categorical syllogism.
D) It aims to prove the truth of a proposition with absolute certainty.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical arguments are defined by their premises—mashhura or conceded—which are persuasive but not absolutely certain, though they can be elevated by divine revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a moral judgment that you consider universally accepted (e.g., 'justice is good'). Is it a mashhura premise? How would you test it using Sharia to see if it becomes yaqin?
?
Find a verse in the Quran that argues from a widely accepted premise (e.g., 'Is God not sufficient for His servant?'). Analyze whether the argument is dialectical or demonstrative.

Dialectic -- Jadal

Forms and Applications of Dialectical Arguments
Lists the possible logical forms and fields of dialectical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which logical form a dialectical argument takes (direct, syllogism, induction, or analogy) and name a field where it is commonly used.

Core Idea
Dialectical arguments can be delivered through any valid inference form, from immediate inference to compound syllogism.

The author explains the various logical structures that dialectical arguments can adopt. They are not limited to one form; they can be direct or indirect, including conversion, contraposition, syllogistic figures, induction, and analogy. This flexibility makes dialectical argument applicable across many Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the logical forms mentioned
The author enumerates: التقابل (contraposition), العكس (conversion), شكل منتج من أشكال القياس الصحيح (valid syllogism), الاستقراء (induction), التمثيل (analogy). Write them down and note that they cover both immediate and mediate inferences.
This list shows dialectical arguments are not chaotic; they follow rules.
2
Distinguish direct and indirect inference
Direct inference (استدلال مباشر) includes conversion and contraposition; indirect includes syllogism, induction, analogy. Identify which items belong to each category.
The author mentions 'في أي طريق من طرق الاستدلال المباشر أو غير المباشر'.
3
Connect each form to an Islamic discipline
For example, syllogism is used in kalam, analogy (tamthil) is used in fiqh (qiyas), induction is used in usul al-fiqh for deriving general rules from cases. Pick one form and find a real example from your knowledge.
The author says these are found in 'قضايا الحق والواجب' etc.
4
Assess the scope of application
Note the fields mentioned: legal, ethical, social, political, judicial, educational. Dialectic is not confined to classroom logic; it pervades all practical reasoning.
This breadth explains why the mutakallimun studied dialectic so thoroughly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الجدلية:
هي الحجة المؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة، تعتقد الجماهير مضمونها اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، فلا يشعر الذهن لأول النظر بأن نقيضه ممكن، أو المؤلفة من مقدمات يُسلّم بها المخاطب، ولكن هذه المقدمات لا ترقى في حقيقة حالها إلى مرتبة اليقين التام.
وتُقدَّم هذه الحجة الجدلية في أي طريق من طرق الاستدلال المباشر أو غير المباشر؛ «في التقابل - في العكس - في شكل منتج من أشكال القياس الصحيح - في الاستقراء - في التمثيل».
وتوجد أمثلتها في كل مجالات الفكر، وتكثر في قضايا الحق والواجب، كالقانونيات، والأخلاقيات، وتكثر أيضاً في الاجتماعيات، والسياسيات، والقضائيات، ومجالات التربية وغير ذلك.
فمن القضايا المشهورة التي تعتقدها الجماهير اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، الأمثلة التالية التي أوردها الإمام الغزالي:
حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام، وإطعام الطعام، وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والأحكام.
وحكمنا بقبح إيذاء الإنسان، وقتل الحيوان، ووضع البهتان، ورضاء الأزواج بفجور النسوان، ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران والطغيان.
ولكن هذا إذا لم نضع في اعتبارنا تعاليم الشرائع الربانية وما ثبت فيها بيقين، أما إذا وضعنا ذلك في اعتبارنا، فإن كثيراً من القضايا المشهورة ترتقي ببيانات الشريعة القاطعة إلى مرتبة اليقين الجازم، فتكون لدى المؤمنين بالشريعة - العارفين لما ثبت فيها بيقين - يقينيات.
ومن أمثلة الحجج الجدلية في القرآن، الاستدلال على ضرورة اليوم الآخر بصفة العدل التي يتصف بها الخالق جلّ وعلا، وأن من مقتضى العدل عدم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، بين الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات والمفسدين في الأرض، بين المتقين والفجار.
قال الله تعالى في سورة (القلم 68):
﴿أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ (35) مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ (36)﴾.
وقال تعالى في سورة (ص 38):
﴿أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ (28)﴾.
وقد جاء هذا رداً على الذين أنكروا اليوم الآخر.
ويمكن أن نصوغ هذا الدليل على الوجه التالي:
لو لم يكن يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء حقيقة ثابتة، لكان واقع هذه الحياة يستلزم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، وهذه التسوية تستلزم نفي صفة العدل عن الخالق جلّ وعلا.
لكن صفة العدل لله ثابتة ∴ فلا تسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين ∴ فلابد من يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء ∴ فهذا اليوم الآخر حقيقة ثابتة
The dialectical argument: It is the argument composed of widely accepted premises, the masses believe their content with belief approximating certainty, such that the mind does not initially feel that its opposite is possible, or composed of premises conceded by the interlocutor, but these premises do not in reality rise to the level of complete certainty. And this dialectical argument is presented in any path of direct or indirect inference: in opposition (contraposition), in inversion, in a productive figure from the valid figures of syllogism, in induction, in analogy. Its examples are found in all fields of thought, and are abundant in issues of truth and duty, such as legal and ethical matters, and also in social, political, judicial, educational fields, and others. Among the widely accepted premises that the masses believe with belief approximating certainty are the following examples cited by Imam al-Ghazali: Our judgment of the goodness of spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, and observing justice in judgments and rulings. And our judgment of the ugliness of harming people, killing animals, slandering, husbands being pleased with their wives' immorality, and responding to blessings with ingratitude and transgression. But this is if we do not take into consideration the teachings of divine laws and what has been established therein with certainty; if we take that into consideration, many of the widely accepted premises rise, by the decisive evidences of the Sharia, to the level of absolute certainty, and thus become certainties for the believers in the Sharia who know what is established therein with certainty. And among the examples of dialectical arguments in the Quran is the inference of the necessity of the Last Day from the attribute of justice with which the Creator is characterized, and that justice requires not treating Muslims and criminals equally, not treating those who believe and do righteous deeds like those who spread corruption on earth, nor the righteous like the wicked. Allah said in Surah al-Qalam (68): ['Afa-nal Muslimina kal-mujrimun? Ma lakum kayfa tahkumun?'] And He said in Surah Sad (38): ['Am naj'alu alladhina amanu wa amilu al-salihati kal-mufsideen fi al-ardi am naj'alu al-muttaqeen kal-fujjar?'] And this came in response to those who denied the Last Day. And we can formulate this proof as follows: If there were no Last Day for reckoning and recompense as a fixed reality, then the reality of this life would necessitate equality between Muslims and criminals, and this equality would necessitate negating the attribute of justice from the Creator. But the attribute of justice for Allah is established. Therefore, there is no equality between Muslims and criminals. Therefore, there must be a Last Day for reckoning and recompense. Therefore, this Last Day is a fixed reality.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the dialectical argument (hujja jadaliyya). It begins by explaining that dialectical arguments are composed of widely accepted premises (mashhura) that the public believes with near-certainty, such that the mind does not immediately conceive of their opposite. Alternatively, they may be composed of premises conceded by the interlocutor, though these premises do not reach the level of complete certainty. The author then lists the various logical forms dialectical arguments can take: contraposition, conversion, valid syllogistic figures, induction, and analogy. Examples from al-Ghazali are given: judgments about the goodness of social virtues and the ugliness of vices. These are widely accepted but not necessarily certain from a religious perspective unless supported by Sharia. The author then provides a Quranic example: arguing for the Last Day based on God's justice. The argument is formalized as a conditional syllogism: if there were no Last Day, then justice would be violated; but God is just; therefore, there must be a Last Day.

Key Takeaway
Dialectical arguments are formally valid inferences (immediate or mediate) that take widely accepted or conceded premises, making them applicable across all practical sciences.

Quranic Dialectical Argument for the Last Day
Provides a Quranic dialectical argument and its formalization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional syllogism (modus tollens) used in the Quran to prove the necessity of the Last Day from God's attribute of justice.

Core Idea
The Quran argues: If no Last Day, God would be unjust; but God is just, therefore the Last Day exists.

The author presents a powerful dialectical argument from the Quran that infers the reality of the Last Day from God's justice. The argument is structured as a conditional syllogism. By analyzing its form, you will see how dialectical arguments can be compelling even when they rely on widely accepted premises rather than absolute certainties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ultimate conclusion
The conclusion is that the Last Day is a fixed reality (حقيقة ثابتة). The author states this at the end of the formalization.
This is the natija that the argument aims to prove.
2
Find the premises
The main premise is that God is just (صفة العدل لله ثابتة). A second premise is that if Muslims and criminals were treated equally in this life, that would negate God's justice. The author also implies that without a Last Day, there would be equality (التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين).
These are taken as widely accepted (mashhura) or conceded by the opponent.
3
Structure the conditional chain
The argument can be expressed as: لو لم يكن يوم آخر (if no Last Day) → لكانت التسوية (then equality) → لكان نفي العدل (then negation of justice). But since justice is affirmed, equality is false, so the Last Day exists. This is a conditional syllogism with two hypotheticals.
Formally: If not G, then E; if E, then not J; J; therefore G.
4
Identify the logical form (modus tollens)
The core inference is: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'no Last Day', Q = 'God is unjust' (or equality). Since justice is affirmed (not Q), the conclusion is that the Last Day exists (not P).
This is a valid modus tollens, but the premises are dialectical.
5
Evaluate the strength of the premises
The premise that God is just is certain for believers but may be contested by deniers. The premise that equality without afterlife implies injustice is taken as obvious (mashhura). So the argument is dialectical, not demonstrative.
The Quranic verses are presented to remind the deniers of this obvious truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الجدلية:
هي الحجة المؤلفة من مقدمات مشهورة، تعتقد الجماهير مضمونها اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، فلا يشعر الذهن لأول النظر بأن نقيضه ممكن، أو المؤلفة من مقدمات يُسلّم بها المخاطب، ولكن هذه المقدمات لا ترقى في حقيقة حالها إلى مرتبة اليقين التام.
وتُقدَّم هذه الحجة الجدلية في أي طريق من طرق الاستدلال المباشر أو غير المباشر؛ «في التقابل - في العكس - في شكل منتج من أشكال القياس الصحيح - في الاستقراء - في التمثيل».
وتوجد أمثلتها في كل مجالات الفكر، وتكثر في قضايا الحق والواجب، كالقانونيات، والأخلاقيات، وتكثر أيضاً في الاجتماعيات، والسياسيات، والقضائيات، ومجالات التربية وغير ذلك.
فمن القضايا المشهورة التي تعتقدها الجماهير اعتقاداً مقارباً لليقين، الأمثلة التالية التي أوردها الإمام الغزالي:
حكمنا بحسن إفشاء السلام، وإطعام الطعام، وصلة الأرحام، وملازمة الصدق في الكلام، ومراعاة العدل في القضايا والأحكام.
وحكمنا بقبح إيذاء الإنسان، وقتل الحيوان، ووضع البهتان، ورضاء الأزواج بفجور النسوان، ومقابلة النعمة بالكفران والطغيان.
ولكن هذا إذا لم نضع في اعتبارنا تعاليم الشرائع الربانية وما ثبت فيها بيقين، أما إذا وضعنا ذلك في اعتبارنا، فإن كثيراً من القضايا المشهورة ترتقي ببيانات الشريعة القاطعة إلى مرتبة اليقين الجازم، فتكون لدى المؤمنين بالشريعة - العارفين لما ثبت فيها بيقين - يقينيات.
ومن أمثلة الحجج الجدلية في القرآن، الاستدلال على ضرورة اليوم الآخر بصفة العدل التي يتصف بها الخالق جلّ وعلا، وأن من مقتضى العدل عدم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، بين الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات والمفسدين في الأرض، بين المتقين والفجار.
قال الله تعالى في سورة (القلم 68):
﴿أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ (35) مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ (36)﴾.
وقال تعالى في سورة (ص 38):
﴿أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ (28)﴾.
وقد جاء هذا رداً على الذين أنكروا اليوم الآخر.
ويمكن أن نصوغ هذا الدليل على الوجه التالي:
لو لم يكن يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء حقيقة ثابتة، لكان واقع هذه الحياة يستلزم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، وهذه التسوية تستلزم نفي صفة العدل عن الخالق جلّ وعلا.
لكن صفة العدل لله ثابتة ∴ فلا تسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين ∴ فلابد من يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء ∴ فهذا اليوم الآخر حقيقة ثابتة
The dialectical argument: It is the argument composed of widely accepted premises, the masses believe their content with belief approximating certainty, such that the mind does not initially feel that its opposite is possible, or composed of premises conceded by the interlocutor, but these premises do not in reality rise to the level of complete certainty. And this dialectical argument is presented in any path of direct or indirect inference: in opposition (contraposition), in inversion, in a productive figure from the valid figures of syllogism, in induction, in analogy. Its examples are found in all fields of thought, and are abundant in issues of truth and duty, such as legal and ethical matters, and also in social, political, judicial, educational fields, and others. Among the widely accepted premises that the masses believe with belief approximating certainty are the following examples cited by Imam al-Ghazali: Our judgment of the goodness of spreading peace, feeding food, maintaining kinship ties, adhering to truthfulness in speech, and observing justice in judgments and rulings. And our judgment of the ugliness of harming people, killing animals, slandering, husbands being pleased with their wives' immorality, and responding to blessings with ingratitude and transgression. But this is if we do not take into consideration the teachings of divine laws and what has been established therein with certainty; if we take that into consideration, many of the widely accepted premises rise, by the decisive evidences of the Sharia, to the level of absolute certainty, and thus become certainties for the believers in the Sharia who know what is established therein with certainty. And among the examples of dialectical arguments in the Quran is the inference of the necessity of the Last Day from the attribute of justice with which the Creator is characterized, and that justice requires not treating Muslims and criminals equally, not treating those who believe and do righteous deeds like those who spread corruption on earth, nor the righteous like the wicked. Allah said in Surah al-Qalam (68): ['Afa-nal Muslimina kal-mujrimun? Ma lakum kayfa tahkumun?'] And He said in Surah Sad (38): ['Am naj'alu alladhina amanu wa amilu al-salihati kal-mufsideen fi al-ardi am naj'alu al-muttaqeen kal-fujjar?'] And this came in response to those who denied the Last Day. And we can formulate this proof as follows: If there were no Last Day for reckoning and recompense as a fixed reality, then the reality of this life would necessitate equality between Muslims and criminals, and this equality would necessitate negating the attribute of justice from the Creator. But the attribute of justice for Allah is established. Therefore, there is no equality between Muslims and criminals. Therefore, there must be a Last Day for reckoning and recompense. Therefore, this Last Day is a fixed reality.

Dialectical Argument in the Quran: Proving the Afterlife
Author provides a Quranic example of dialectical argument and formulates it as a syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a dialectical argument in the Quran and formulate it as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i) in modus tollens form.

Core Idea
The Quran uses dialectical arguments to compel belief by starting from accepted premises.

In this lesson, we examine a Quranic argument for the afterlife that is dialectical in nature. The author illustrates how the Quran uses God's attribute of justice as a premise that the denier would accept, then builds a conditional argument that forces the conclusion. We will see the verses and then the formal syllogistic reconstruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared premise
The argument uses God's justice, which both the believer and the denier of the afterlife would accept as Muslims. This is key to dialectical argument: starting from premises the opponent already accepts.
The premise of God's justice is taken from Islamic belief, not from observation.
2
Find the Quranic verses
Look at Qalam 35-36 and Sad 28. They ask rhetorical questions: 'Shall We make the Muslims like the criminals?' implying that justice requires non-equality.
The verses are implicit premises in the argument.
3
Trace the conditional structure
The author formulates: If there were no afterlife, then believers and criminals would be equal (since no final reckoning). But equality would negate God's justice. God's justice is established, so the denial of the consequent leads to denial of the antecedent – the afterlife must exist.
This is a valid modus tollens.
4
Verify logical validity
Check that the syllogism is valid: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P. Here P = 'no afterlife', Q = 'equality between believers and criminals'. The negation of Q (no equality) is derived from God's justice.
The argument is sound if the premises (especially God's justice) are accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أمثلة الحجج الجدلية في القرآن، الاستدلال على ضرورة اليوم الآخر بصفة العدل التي يتصف بها الخالق جلّ وعلا، وأن من مقتضى العدل عدم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، بين الذين آمنوا وعملوا الصالحات والمفسدين في الأرض، بين المتقين والفجار. قال الله تعالى في سورة (القلم ٦٨): ﴿أَفَنَجْعَلُ الْمُسْلِمِينَ كَالْمُجْرِمِينَ (٣٥) مَا لَكُمْ كَيْفَ تَحْكُمُونَ (٣٦)﴾. وقال تعالى في سورة (ص ٣٨): ﴿أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ كَالْمُفْسِدِينَ فِي الْأَرْضِ أَمْ نَجْعَلُ الْمُتَّقِينَ كَالْفُجَّارِ (٢٨)﴾. وقد جاء هذا رداً على الذين أنكروا اليوم الآخر. ويمكن أن نصوغ هذا الدليل على الوجه التالي: لو لم يكن يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء حقيقة ثابتة، لكان واقع هذه الحياة يستلزم التسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين، وهذه التسوية تستلزم نفي صفة العدل عن الخالق جلّ وعلا. لكن صفة العدل لله ثابتة ∴ فلا تسوية بين المسلمين والمجرمين ∴ فلابد من يوم آخر للحساب والجزاء ∴ فهذا اليوم الآخر حقيقة ثابتة
Among examples of dialectical arguments in the Qur'an is inferring the necessity of the Last Day from the attribute of justice with which the Creator, Exalted and Majestic, is characterized, and that from the requirement of justice is non-equality between Muslims and criminals, between those who believed and did righteous deeds and those who cause corruption in the land, between the righteous and the wicked. Allah Almighty said in Surah Al-Qalam (68): 'Then shall We make the Muslims like the criminals? (35) What is the matter with you? How do you judge? (36).' And He said in Surah Sad (38): 'Or shall We make those who believe and do righteous deeds like those who cause corruption in the land? Or shall We make the righteous like the wicked? (28).' This came as a response to those who denied the Last Day. This proof can be formulated as follows: If there were not a Last Day for reckoning and recompense as a true reality, then the reality of this life would necessitate equality between Muslims and criminals, and this equality would necessitate negating the attribute of justice from the Creator, Exalted and Majestic. But the attribute of justice for Allah is established, ∴ there is no equality between Muslims and criminals, ∴ there must be a Last Day for reckoning and recompense, ∴ this Last Day is a true reality.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is an illustration of a dialectical argument (hujja jadaliyya) taken from the Qur'an. The author first identifies the argument: it is an inference (istidlal) to establish the necessity of the Last Day. The key premises are: God is just, and justice requires treating believers and criminals differently. The verses cited (Qalam 35-36 and Sad 28) are presented as rhetorical questions that implicitly assert this non-equality. The author then formalizes the argument as a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): If there were no afterlife, then believers and criminals would be treated equally in this life, which would negate God's justice. But God's justice is established, so the antecedent is false, implying the reality of the afterlife. This is a classic modus tollens structure. The argument is dialectical because it relies on a premise (God's justice) that the opponent (the denier of the afterlife) would accept as a Muslim, thereby compelling them to accept the conclusion.

الحجة الجدلية
اليوم الآخر
صفة العدل

D) A rhetorical argument based on analogy

Key Takeaway
Dialectical arguments in the Quran often use a conditional form (modus tollens) to derive necessary beliefs from accepted premises.

?
Task 2: Using the same structure (modus tollens), construct a valid argument on the topic of divine unity (tawhid) starting from a premise accepted by your opponent.

Navigating the Fifth Part on Debate Controls
Author lists the chapters and topics of the fifth part on debate etiquettes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two main chapters and ten subtopics of the fifth part on debate controls and relate them to the broader discipline of munazara.

Core Idea
This table of contents is your roadmap to mastering the art of Islamic debate.

Before diving into the rules and applications of debate, it helps to see the full landscape. This passage lists the two chapters and ten subtopics of the fifth part, covering everything from general preliminaries to specific controls and Qur'anic examples. Use this lesson to orient yourself and understand what each section contributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two chapters
The passage states that the fifth part contains two chapters (فصلان). Locate them: Chapter One on general prelminaries and Chapter Two on specific controls and applications.
Each chapter groups related topics.
2
Examine Chapter One topics
List the six topics: debating in the best manner, origin of the art, etiquettes of debaters, pillars, stages, and scope of debate. These are foundational.
These set the ethical and structural framework.
3
Examine Chapter Two topics
List the ten topics: debate in expression, transmission, definitions, divisions, assents, incomplete composites, prohibitions, conclusion, terms, and Qur'anic debates. These are practical and applied.
Note the progression from simple to complex and the inclusion of scriptural examples.
4
Relate to the science of munazara
Each topic corresponds to a part of debate methodology. For instance, 'debate in definitions' connects to hadd and rasm, while 'assents' (tasdiqat) deals with propositions and syllogisms.
This outline shows the comprehensive coverage of munazara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القِسْمُ الخَامِسُ
ضَوَابِطُ المُنَاظَرَةِ وَآدَابِهَا
وفيه فصلان :
الفصل الأول : مقدمات عامة :
١ - البحث والجدال بالتي هي أحسن .
٢ - نشأة فنّ آداب البحث والمناظرة .
٣ - آداب المتناظرين .
٤ - أركان المناظرة .
٥ - مراحل المناظرة .
٦ - ما تجري فيه المناظرة وما لا تجري فيه .
الفصل الثاني : ضوابط المناظرة وتطبيقات :
١ - المناظرة في العبارة .
٢ - المناظرة في النقل .
٣ - المناظرة في التعريفات .
٤ - التقسيمات والمناظرة فيها .
٥ - التصديقات والمناظرة فيها .
٦ - المناظرة في المركب الناقص وفي قيود القضايا .
٧ - ما لا يجوز للمناظر أن يرتكبه في المناظرة .
٨ - انتهاء المناظرة .
٩ - مصطلحات .
١٠ - مناظرات قرآنية .
The Fifth Part: Controls of Debate and its Etiquettes. It contains two chapters: Chapter One: General Preliminaries: 1 - Research and debate in the best manner. 2 - Origin of the art of research and debate. 3 - Etiquettes of the debaters. 4 - Pillars of debate. 5 - Stages of debate. 6 - What debate is valid for and what it is not valid for. Chapter Two: Controls of Debate and Applications: 1 - Debate in expression. 2 - Debate in transmission. 3 - Debate in definitions. 4 - Divisions and debate in them. 5 - Assents (tasdiqat) and debate in them. 6 - Debate in incomplete compositum and in propositional restrictions. 7 - What it is not permissible for the debater to commit in debate. 8 - Conclusion of debate. 9 - Terms. 10 - Qur'anic debates.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is the table of contents for the fifth part of the book, titled 'Controls of Debate and its Etiquettes' (Dawabit al-munazara wa adabuha). It outlines two chapters. Chapter One covers general preliminary topics such as the command to debate in the best manner, the historical origin of the art of debate, the etiquette of the two debaters, the pillars of debate (which typically include the disputants, the subject, the claim, etc.), the stages of debate, and the scope of debate—what topics are permissible to debate and what are not. Chapter Two delves into specific controls and applications: debating about wording (expression), debating about transmitted reports (naql), debating about definitions, divisions and debating them, debating about assents (tasdiqat) and propositions, debating about incomplete composites and propositional restrictions, prohibitions during debate, how to conclude a debate, a glossary of terms, and finally examples of Qur'anic debates. This structure shows the comprehensive treatment of the subject, combining theoretical principles with practical examples.

Key Terms
مناظرة (munāẓara): Debate; a formal intellectual disputation following specific rules. [Note: Standard term for dialectical debate.]
ضوابط (ḍawābiṭ): Controls; rules or regulations that govern debate. [Note: Used in books on adab al-bahth.]
مقدمات عامة (muqaddimāt ʿāmma): General prelminaries or introductory matters. [Note: Standard.]
آداب (ādāb): Etiquettes; proper conduct in debate. [Note: Standard.]
تصديقات (taṣdīqāt): Assents; propositions that can be affirmed or denied, as opposed to tasawwurat (conceptions). [Note: Standard logical term.]

Structure of the Fifth Section: Controls of Dialectical Debate
Outlines the table of contents for the section on the controls and manners of dialectical debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to map the overall structure of the fifth section and identify the specific topics covered in each of its two chapters.

Core Idea
The controls and manners of munazara are systematically organised into foundational principles and applied rules.

Before diving into the details of dialectical debate, it is essential to grasp the overall architecture of the section. This outline serves as your roadmap: it shows you the progression from general introductions to specific controls, and helps you anticipate the logical flow of the material. Understanding this structure will allow you to place each subsequent lesson within its proper context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the section title and its two chapters
The section is called 'Controls of Dialectical Debate and Its Manners'. It contains two chapters. Note the Arabic term 'فصلان' which means 'two chapters'.
Remember: 'فصل' (fasl) = chapter.
2
List the topics of the first chapter
The first chapter is 'General Introductions' (مقدمات عامة). It covers six topics: the nature of inquiry and debate, history of the art, manners of the disputants, pillars, stages, and the scope of debate.
These provide the foundational concepts before specific controls.
3
List the topics of the second chapter
The second chapter is 'Controls of Dialectical Debate and Applications' (ضوابط المناظرة وتطبيقات). It has ten subsections, from debate on expression and transmission to Quranic examples.
This chapter applies the general principles to concrete areas.
4
Trace the progression from general to specific
Notice how the first chapter sets the stage with general introductions, while the second delves into specific controls and applications. This is a common pedagogical structure in Islamic sciences: foundations first, then detailed rules.
This structure helps in memorising and understanding the material.

The Fifth Section: Controls of Dialectical Debate and Its Manners. And it contains two chapters: The First Chapter: General Introductions: 1 - Inquiry and debate in the best manner. 2 - The emergence of the art of the manners of inquiry and dialectical debate. 3 - Manners of the two disputants. 4 - Pillars of dialectical debate. 5 - Stages of dialectical debate. 6 - What dialectical debate pertains to and what it does not pertain to. Commentary
This passage is a table of contents for the fifth major section of the book, which deals with the controls and manners of dialectical debate (munazara). The author first states the title: 'al-Qism al-Khamis: Dawabit al-Munazara wa Adabiha'. Then he notes that this section contains two chapters (fannan). He then lists the first chapter, 'al-Fasl al-Awwal: Muqaddimat 'Amma', which includes six subsections on general introductions, covering the nature of inquiry and debate, the history of the art, manners of disputants, pillars, stages, and the scope of debate. Next, the second chapter, 'al-Fasl al-Thani: Dawabit al-Munazara wa Tatbiqat', enumerates ten more specific subsections on the application of controls to various aspects of debate, such as expression, transmission, definitions, divisions, assents (tasdiqat), incomplete compounds, prohibitions, conclusion, terminology, and finally Quranic examples of debate. The page number '359' appears at the end, likely indicating the original book page. This outline gives the student a clear structure for the upcoming material, which will be elaborated in subsequent pages.

Defining Jadal: The Islamic Art of Truth-Seeking Debate
Author defines jadal and its Islamic foundation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan from mere argumentation and identify its rules and purpose.

Core Idea
Islamic dialectic is not combat but cooperative illumination—two seekers helping each other find truth.

This passage introduces the concept of jadal as an Islamic tool for research and persuasion. Unlike modern polemics, jadal is a disciplined dialogue with the goal of reaching truth together. The author grounds it in Quranic commands and defines its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of jadal
Read the definition of jadal and note that its primary goal is cooperation to reach truth, not victory.
The text says: 'هدف المناظرة هو في الأصل تعاون الفريقين المتناظرين على معرفة الحقيقة والتوصل إليها'.
2
Extract the conditions
List the conditions for permissible debate: mutual understanding, presenting evidence, openness to criticism, absence of fanaticism and violence.
Conditions are described in the first two paragraphs.
3
Recognize the Quranic basis
Identify the verses from Surah An-Nahl and Al-Ankabut that command arguing in the best way.
The phrase 'بالتي هي أحسن' is the key.
4
Apply to a real debate
Consider the example of Prophet Ibrahim (AS) and how his arguments with his people illustrate jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan.
The passage cites Surah Al-An'am 83.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
مُقَدِّمَاتٌ عَامَّةٌ
(١)
البَحْثُ وَالجِدَالُ بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ
قد تدعو حاجة البحث المشترك للتوصل إلى الحق، وحاجة الإقناع بالحق، إلى استخدام وسيلة (المناظرة)، وهي الجدال بالتي هي أحسن.
وللمناظرة المأذون بها شروط وقواعد وأصول ينبغي اتباعها، صيانة لها عن أن تتحول إلى مماراة بعيدة عن نشدان الحقيقة، أو إلى مشاحنات أنانية، ومشاتمات ومغالطات، ونحو ذلك مما يفسد القلوب، ويهيج النفوس، ويورث التعصب، ولا يوصل إلى حق.
والجدال: هو حوار كلامي يتفهم فيه كل طرف من الفريقين المتحاورين وجهة نظر الطرف الآخر، ويعرض فيه كل طرف منهما أدلته التي رجحت لديه استمساكه بوجهة نظره، ثم يأخذ بتبصر الحقيقة من خلال الانتقادات التي يوجهها الطرف الآخر على أدلته، أو من خلال الأدلة التي يُنير له بها بعض النقاط التي كانت غامضة عليه.
وهدف المناظرة هو في الأصل تعاون الفريقين المتناظرين على معرفة الحقيقة والتوصل إليها، بتبصير كل منهما صاحبه بالأماكن المظلمة عليه، والتي خفيت عنه حينما أخذ ينظر باحثاً عن الحقيقة، وذلك حينما لا يكون أحدهما واقفاً على الحقيقة المبينة وقوفاً قطعياً غير قابل للنقض، أما في هذه الحالة فإن هدف المناظرة إنما هو تبصير الواقف على الحقيقة أخاه المناظر له بها، والأخذ بيده في طرق الاستدلال الصحيح لإبلاغه وجه الحق المشرق، وذلك باستخدام الحوار البريء من التعصب، الخالي من العنف والانفعال، المتمشي وفق الأصول العامة للحوار الذي يهدف فيه كل من الفريقين المتحاورين أن يصل إلى الحقيقة، كأنه جاهل بها، خالي الذهن والنفس من أي استمساك سابق بوجهة نظر معينة من وجهات النظر المختلفة، وذلك للابتعاد عن كل أجواء التعصب والأنانية، التي تصرف النفوس والأفكار عن تفهم الحق، أو التسليم به، ولو انكشف لها واضحاً جلياً.
الجدال بالتي هي أحسن من وسائل الدعوة إلى الإسلام:
ولما كان الجدال في الواقع الإنساني من الوسائل التي تستخدم لنشر الأفكار والإقناع بها، وكان الإنسان أكثر شيء جدلاً، والجدال سلاحه للدفاع عن أفكاره، فقد أمر الإسلام به للدفاع عن الحق الإسلامي مشروطاً بأن يكون بالتي هي أحسن، كما أمر بالقتال لصد عدوان الكافرين، فقال الله تعالى لنبيه محمد ﷺ في سورة (النحل ١٢٥):
﴿وَجَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ١٢٥﴾.
وخاطب الله تعالى المؤمنين في سورة (العنكبوت ٤٦) بقوله:
﴿وَلَا تُجَدِلُوا أَهْلَ الْكِتَبِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ٤٦﴾.
أي: فإن سلك مجادلوكم مسالك غير مهذبة القول، فتقيدوا أنتم بكل قول مهذب، واسلكوا كل طريقة هي أحسن وأفضل. وعبارة «بالتي هي أحسن» تشمل بعمومها الأساليب الفكرية والقولية، وبهذا يتبين لنا أن المطلوب من المسلم أن يكون في مجادلته، على حالة أرقى وأحسن باستمرار من الحالة التي يكون عليها من يجادله، أدباً وتهذيباً، أو قولاً وفكراً.
وقد آتى الله إبراهيم عليه السلام قوة حجة، وقدرة على الجدال للإلزام بالحق، وهذا ما تكشفه لنا جدلياته التي قص القرآن علينا طرفاً منها، وأثنى الله على قوة حجة إبراهيم عليه السلام بقوله في سورة (الأنعام ٨٣):
﴿وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا ءَاتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ نَرْفَعُ دَرَجَاتٍ مَّن نَّشَاءُ إِنَّ رَبَّكَ حَكِيمٌ عَلِيمٌ ٨٣﴾.
First Chapter
General Introductions
(1)
Research and Dialectic in the Best Way
Often the need for joint research to reach the truth, and the need to persuade with the truth, call for using the means of (debate), which is dialectic in the best way.
And for permissible debate there are conditions, rules, and principles that should be followed, to protect it from turning into a wrangling far from the pursuit of truth, or into selfish quarrels, insults, fallacies, and the like that corrupt hearts, excite souls, engender fanaticism, and do not lead to the truth.
Dialectic is a verbal dialogue in which each party of the two debating sides understands the other's point of view, and each of them presents the evidence that has made him lean towards holding his point of view, then he proceeds to perceive the truth through the criticisms that the other side directs at his evidence, or through the evidence that illuminates for him some points that were obscure to him.
The goal of debate originally is the cooperation of the two debating parties to know and reach the truth, by each enlightening his companion about the dark spots that were hidden from him when he began to search for the truth, and that is when one of them is not standing on the clear truth with a definitive stance that is not open to refutation. As for this case, the goal of debate is only to enlighten the one standing on the truth his debating brother, and to take his hand in the paths of correct inference to convey to him the bright face of truth, by using dialogue free from fanaticism, devoid of violence and emotion, proceeding according to the general principles of dialogue in which each of the two debating parties aims to reach the truth, as if he were ignorant of it, with his mind and soul free from any prior adherence to a particular viewpoint among different viewpoints, in order to stay away from all atmospheres of fanaticism and selfishness that turn souls and thoughts away from understanding the truth or submitting to it, even if it becomes clearly manifest to them.
Dialectic in the best way is among the means of calling to Islam:
And since dialectic in human reality is among the means used to spread ideas and persuade with them, and man is the most argumentative of things, and dialectic is his weapon to defend his ideas, Islam commanded it to defend the Islamic truth, conditioned that it be in the best way, just as it commanded fighting to repel the aggression of the disbelievers. Allah Almighty said to His Prophet Muhammad ﷺ in Surah (An-Nahl 125):
{And argue with them in the best way 125}.
And Allah Almighty addressed the believers in Surah (Al-Ankabut 46) saying:
{And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way 46}.
That is: If your arguers follow impolite ways of speech, then you abide by every polite saying, and follow every way that is better and best. And the phrase 'in the best way' generally includes intellectual and verbal methods. By this it becomes clear to us that what is required of the Muslim in his argument is to be in a state that is constantly higher and better than the state of the one he argues with, in terms of politeness and refinement, or speech and thought.
And Allah gave Abraham, peace be upon him, strength of argument and ability to argue to compel the truth, and this is revealed to us by his debates that the Quran narrated a part of, and Allah praised the strength of Abraham's argument in His saying in Surah (Al-An'am 83):
{And that is Our argument which We gave Abraham against his people; We raise in degrees whom We will; indeed your Lord is Wise, Knowing 83}.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is an introductory discussion on the concept of jadal (dialectic) and its role in Islamic discourse. The author begins by stating that the need for joint research to reach truth and persuasion necessitates the use of munazara (debate), which is defined as jadal in the best way. The passage then outlines the conditions for permissible debate: it must have rules and principles to prevent it from devolving into mere wrangling, selfish quarrels, insults, and fallacies that corrupt hearts and lead to fanaticism.

Next, the author provides a precise definition of jadal: it is a verbal dialogue where each side understands the other's viewpoint, presents its evidence, and then perceives the truth through criticisms from the other side or through evidence that clarifies obscure points. The goal of munazara is cooperation between the two parties to reach the truth, with each enlightening the other on blind spots. However, if one party already possesses definitive truth, the goal becomes guiding the other to that truth through a dialogue free from fanaticism, violence, and emotion, as if both are ignorant, to avoid bias.

The author then ties this concept to Islamic da'wah, citing Quranic verses from Surah An-Nahl (125) and Al-Ankabut (46) that command arguing in the best way. The phrase 'in the best way' encompasses both intellectual and verbal methods, requiring the Muslim to always be at a higher level of politeness, refinement, speech, and thought than the opponent. Finally, the author mentions Prophet Ibrahim (Abraham) as an example given strength of argument to compel truth, quoting Surah Al-An'am (83).

Key Terms
الجدال
المناظرة
الحوار

Jadal Billati Hiya Ahsan: Definition and Quranic Basis
Author defines jadal bilati hiya ahsan, cites Quranic proofs, and distinguishes praiseworthy from blameworthy debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan and distinguish it from blameworthy jadal using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Jadal bilati hiya ahsan is a Quranically-mandated ethical framework for debate aimed at truth, not victory.

This lesson unpacks the Islamic concept of jadal (debate) as a divine tool for da'wa. The author provides a definition grounded in Quranic imperatives and prophetic examples, establishing a clear ethical boundary between debate that serves truth and debate that serves ego.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of jadal bilati hiya ahsan
Read the first paragraph. Identify the key conditions: both parties must aim for truth, be free from prior bias, and avoid fanaticism. Notice the phrase 'as if ignorant of it' to ensure open-mindedness.
This is a descriptive definition (rasm), not a strict genus-differentia hadd.
2
Locate the Quranic commands for debate
Find the verses from Surah an-Nahl (125) and Surah al-Ankabut (46). Note the phrase 'in the best way' (bi-llati hiya ahsan) and how it applies to both the Prophet and ordinary believers. Consider the contexts: da'wa to polytheists and dialogue with People of the Book.
3
Analyze prophetic examples of ideal debate
Read the examples of Ibrahim and Nuh. Ibrahim was given 'strong argument'; Nuh showed patience even when opponents became weary. Identify qualities: strength of proof, patience, persistence without aggression.
The Quran praises Ibrahim's argument and records Nuh's long debate.
4
Distinguish praiseworthy from blameworthy debate
Find the concluding contrast: debate for truth (may be obligatory) vs. debate for personal victory (may be forbidden). Note the term 'khisam' for quarrelsome disputation without clarity, as in Surah az-Zukhruf (18).
Blameworthy jadal obscures truth and seeks dominance.
5
Apply the framework to a modern scenario
Consider a hypothetical debate about the permissibility of riba. How would a Muslim practicing jadal bilati hiya ahsan conduct it? Check for sincerity, politeness, and focus on truth rather than winning.

المتمشي وفق الأصول العامة للحوار الذي يهدف فيه كل من الفريقين المتحاورين أن يصل إلى الحقيقة، كأنه جاهل بها، خالي الذهن والنفس من أي استمساك سابق بوجهة نظر معينة من وجهات النظر المختلفة، وذلك للابتعاد عن كل أجواء التعصب والأنانية، التي تصرف النفوس والأفكار عن تفهم الحق، أو التسليم به، ولو انكشف لها واضحاً جلياً.
الجدال بالتي هي أحسن من وسائل الدعوة إلى الإسلام:
ولما كان الجدال في الواقع الإنساني من الوسائل التي تستخدم لنشر الأفكار والإقناع بها، وكان الإنسان أكثر شيء جدلاً، والجدال سلاحه للدفاع عن أفكاره، فقد أمر الإسلام به للدفاع عن الحق الإسلامي مشروطاً بأن يكون بالتي هي أحسن، كما أمر بالقتال لصد عدوان الكافرين، فقال الله تعالى لنبيه محمد ﷺ في سورة (النحل ١٢٥): ﴿وَجَدِلْهُم بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ١٢٥﴾.
وخاطب الله تعالى المؤمنين في سورة (العنكبوت ٤٦) بقوله: ﴿وَلَا تُجَدِلُوا أَهْلَ الْكِتَبِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ٤٦﴾.
أي: فإن سلك مجادلوكم مسالك غير مهذبة القول، فتقيدوا أنتم بكل قول مهذب، واسلكوا كل طريقة هي أحسن وأفضل. وعبارة «بالتي هي أحسن» تشمل بعمومها الأساليب الفكرية والقولية، وبهذا يتبين لنا أن المطلوب من المسلم أن يكون في مجادلته، على حالة أرقى وأحسن باستمرار من الحالة التي يكون عليها من يجادله، أدباً وتهذيباً، أو قولاً وفكراً.
وقد آتى الله إبراهيم عليه السلام قوة حجة، وقدرة على الجدال للإلزام بالحق، وهذا ما تكشفه لنا جدلياته التي قص القرآن علينا طرفاً منها، وأثنى الله على قوة حجة إبراهيم عليه السلام بقوله في سورة (الأنعام ٨٣): ﴿وَتِلْكَ حُجَّتُنَا ءَاتَيْنَهَا إِبْرَاهِيمَ عَلَى قَوْمِهِ نَرْفَعُ دَرَجَاتٍ مَّن نَّشَاءُ إِنَّ رَبَّكَ حَكِيمٌ عَلِيمٌ ٨٣﴾.
وآتى الله نوحاً عليه السلام نفساً طويلاً في جدال الكافرين، لإقناعهم بالحق الذي جاء به من عند الله، حتى ضاقوا ذرعاً بقوة حجته وطول نفسه في الجدال، فقالوا له: يا نوح قد جادلتنا فأكثرت جدالنا، وهذا ما قصه الله علينا في سورة (هود ۱۱) بقوله تعالى: ﴿ قَالُوا يَنُوحُ قَدْ جَدَلْتَنَا فَأَكْثَرْتَ جِدَلَنَا فَأْتِنَا بِمَا تَعِدُنَا إِن كُنتَ مِنَ الصَّدِقِينَ (٣٢) قَالَ إِنَّمَا يَأْتِيكُم بِهِ اللَّهُ إِن شَاءَ وَمَا أَنتُم بِمُعْجِزِينَ (٣٣) ﴾.
وقلل القرآن من قيمة من هو في الخصام غير مبين، فقال الله تعالى في سورة (الزخرف ٤٣): ﴿ أَوَمَن يُنَشَّؤُا فِي الْحِلْيَةِ وَهُوَ فِي الْخِصَامِ غَيْرُ مُبِينٍ (١٨) ﴾.
ومن هذا يتحصل لدينا أن الجدال للوقوف على الحق، أو لإقناع الناس به، عمل محمود، وقد يكون واجباً كالقتال في سبيل الله. أما الجدال انتصاراً للنفس، ورغبة بالاستعلاء والغلبة، فهو عمل مذموم، وقد يكون حراماً إذا كان فيه طمس للحق أو تضليل للمناظر.
Proceeding according to the general principles of dialogue in which each of the two debating parties aims to arrive at the truth, as if ignorant of it, the mind and soul free from any prior attachment to a particular viewpoint among different viewpoints, in order to avoid any atmosphere of fanaticism and selfishness that turn souls and thoughts away from understanding the truth or accepting it, even if it becomes clearly evident to them.
Debate in the best manner (jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan) is among the means of calling to Islam:
Since debate in human reality is among the means used to spread ideas and convince others, and humans are most given to argument, and debate is their weapon to defend their ideas, Islam commanded it for defending the Islamic truth, conditioned that it be in the best manner, just as it commanded fighting to repel the aggression of the disbelievers. So Allah the Exalted said to His Prophet Muhammad ﷺ in Surat an-Nahl (125): "And argue with them in the best way."
And Allah the Exalted addressed the believers in Surat al-Ankabut (46): "And do not argue with the People of the Book except in the best way."
Meaning: If your disputants follow impolite ways of speech, then you adhere to every polite saying, and follow every way that is best and superior. And the phrase "in the best way" encompasses in its generality intellectual and verbal methods, and thereby it becomes clear to us that what is required of the Muslim is that he be in his debate, in a state continuously higher and better than the state of the one he debates, in manners and politeness, or in speech and thought.
And Allah gave Ibrahim (peace be upon him) strength of proof and ability in debate to compel the truth, and this is revealed to us by his debates, some of which the Quran has narrated to us, and Allah praised the strength of Ibrahim's proof in Surat al-An'am (83): "And that was Our argument which We gave Ibrahim against his people. We raise in degrees whom We will. Indeed, your Lord is Wise, Knowing."
And Allah gave Nuh (peace be upon him) great patience in debating the disbelievers, to convince them of the truth he brought from Allah, until they became weary of the strength of his proof and his great patience in debate, so they said to him: "O Nuh, you have argued with us and multiplied your argument with us." This Allah has narrated to us in Surat Hud (11): "They said, 'O Nuh, you have argued with us and multiplied your argument with us, so bring us what you threaten us, if you are of the truthful.' He said, 'Allah will only bring it to you if He wills, and you will not escape.'"
And the Quran belittled the worth of one who is in disputation (khisam) without clarity, as Allah said in Surat az-Zukhruf (43): "Or is one who is brought up in ornaments and is in disputation without clarity?"
From this we derive that debate for the purpose of establishing the truth or convincing people of it is a praiseworthy act, and it may be obligatory like fighting in the way of Allah. As for debate for the victory of oneself and desire for dominance and superiority, it is a blameworthy act, and it may be forbidden if it involves obscuring the truth or misleading the opponent.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage defines the Islamic concept of jadal (debate) specifically as 'jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan' (debate in the best manner). It begins by describing the ideal mindset: both parties should approach the dialogue as if ignorant, free from bias, seeking truth. Then it explains that debate is a natural human tool for spreading ideas; Islam commands it for defending truth but with a condition of excellence. The author cites two Quranic verses: Surat an-Nahl 125 commanding the Prophet to argue in the best way, and Surat al-Ankabut 46 addressing believers regarding the People of the Book. The phrase 'in the best way' is interpreted to include both intellectual and verbal manners, requiring the Muslim to always be in a higher state of civility and thought. The passage then gives examples of prophets: Ibrahim, who was given strength of proof, and Nuh, who patiently debated the disbelievers until they became weary. Finally, the author contrasts praiseworthy debate (for truth's sake, possibly obligatory) with blameworthy debate (for personal victory, potentially forbidden if it obscures truth). This establishes a clear ethical framework for jadal in the Islamic tradition.

Jadal bilati hiya ahsan is a Quranically-mandated ethical framework for debate aimed at truth, free from bias and ego, which can be obligatory in defense of Islamic truth.

Two General Rules for Debating in the Best Manner
Author enumerates the first two rules for Islamic debate: avoiding partisanship and using polite speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Quranic rules for Islamic debate and explain how they relate to the concept of jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan.

Core Idea
Islamic debate requires abandoning prejudice and speaking politely, as commanded in the Quran.

This passage presents two foundational rules for Islamic dialectical debate derived directly from the Quran. These rules are not merely advisory; they are essential for any Muslim engaged in argumentation to establish truth. Dr. 'Abd al-Karim al-Mutairi, after reflecting on Islamic texts and logical principles, deduced these rules as part of a broader methodology for debate in the best manner. They serve as a moral and intellectual framework for all subsequent discussions in Islamic dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of jadal bi-llati hiya ahsan
The Quranic phrase 'debate in the best manner' sets the tone for all Islamic argumentation. It signifies a method that is respectful, fair, and truth-seeking. Recognize that the author uses this phrase as the overarching principle for the rules that follow.
2
Identify the first rule: abandoning prejudice
The first rule states that both parties in a debate must abandon prejudice for their prior viewpoints and declare readiness to accept the truth wherever it appears. The author cites Surah Saba' verse 24 where Muslims tell polytheists: 'We or you are upon guidance or in clear error.' This verse exemplifies complete impartiality.
The rule is about intellectual sincerity, not relativism.
3
Identify the second rule: polite speech
The second rule requires both parties to adhere to polite speech, avoiding insults, defamation, mockery, or contempt. The author supports this with two verses: 'Argue with them in a way that is best' (an-Nahl 16:125) and the instruction to argue with the People of the Book except in the best manner (al-Ankabut 29:46).
Polite speech includes words, thoughts, and actions.
4
Connect the rules to the broader discipline of munazara
These rules are the ethical foundation of Islamic dialectical debate (munazara). In classical Islamic scholarship, munazara was governed by strict adab (etiquette) to ensure that the pursuit of truth remained paramount. Understanding these rules helps you participate in or analyze any Islamic debate.
These are the first two of several rules the author derives.

Islamic guidelines to the general rules for debating in the best manner: We have been able, by reflecting on Islamic texts, Quranic dialectical applications, and evident logical principles, to deduce the following rules: The first rule: Both parties engaging in dialectical dialogue (debate) on a particular topic must abandon prejudice for their prior viewpoint, and declare full readiness to search for the truth and accept it when it appears, whether it is their prior viewpoint, the viewpoint of their interlocutor in the debate, or another viewpoint. The Noble Qur'an has guided us to adopt this rule, as it taught the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him, and the Muslims in Surah Saba' (34) to say to the polytheists in their debate with them: 'And indeed, we or you are either upon guidance or in clear error.' In this is the utmost abandonment of prejudice for a prior matter, and complete declaration of the desire to seek the truth wherever it may be. And since the subject of the debate in which this verse was revealed is the oneness of the Creator or associating partners with Him—two opposites with no meeting between them, and they revolve around a great foundation of religious creed—it was evident that guidance lies in one of them (truth) and clear error in the other (falsehood). Therefore, the statement of abandonment of prejudice for a prior matter implies acknowledgment of this fact. The second rule: Both parties in dialogue must adhere to polite speech, far from any insult, defamation, mockery, sarcasm, or contempt for the viewpoint claimed or defended by the interlocutor. Islam has guided us to adhere to this rule in many texts, including the saying of Allah the Exalted to His Prophet in Surah an-Nahl (16): 'And argue with them in a way that is best.' And His saying, the Exalted, to the believers in Surah al-Ankabut (29): 'And do not argue with the People of the Book except in a way that is best, except those who have wronged among them, and say: We believe in that which has been revealed to us and revealed to you, and our God and your God is one, and we are Muslims to Him.' The guidance to the best plan includes everything related to debate, connected to it, accompanying and associated with it—speech, thought, and action. The Muslim is required to adhere in his debate to prove the truth he believes in and to convince people of it, the plan that is best than any plan people could adopt in their debates. Therefore, it is from...
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents the first two general rules for Islamic dialectical debate (jadal) that are deduced from Quranic texts, dialectical applications, and basic logical principles. The author introduces the rules as 'Islamic guidelines to the general rules for debating in the best manner.' The first rule mandates that both parties in a debate must abandon prejudice for their prior opinions and declare their full readiness to seek the truth wherever it appears, whether it aligns with their initial view, their opponent's view, or a different view altogether. The Qur'anic verse from Surah Saba' (34) is cited as an example: the believers were instructed to tell the polytheists that either they or the polytheists are upon guidance or in clear error. This statement embodies the abandonment of prejudice and the sincere desire for truth. The author notes that since the topic is tawhid vs. shirk—two stark opposites—the verse implies recognition of one as truth and the other as falsehood, yet the phrasing still distances from arrogance. The second rule requires both parties to use polite speech, avoiding insults, defamation, mockery, sarcasm, or contempt for the opponent's viewpoint. Two Quranic verses support this: the command to argue 'in the best way' (Surah an-Nahl) and the instruction not to argue with People of the Book except in the best manner (Surah al-Ankabut). The author then generalizes that 'the best way' encompasses all aspects of debate—speech, thought, and action—obligating the Muslim to adopt the best possible plan when arguing to establish truth and persuade others. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main rules are clearly stated.

The First Rule of Dialectic: Abandoning Partisanship
Author deduces the first rule of debate from Surah Saba, calling for abandonment of prior bias.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the first rule of Islamic dialectic from Surah Saba and explain its role in ensuring objective truth-seeking in debate.

Core Idea
True debate begins by admitting you might be wrong.

The foundation of any productive intellectual exchange is intellectual honesty. The Qur'an itself provides the model: when debating polytheists, the believers are instructed to say, 'Indeed, either we or you are upon guidance or in manifest error' (Saba: 24). This verse is not a statement of relativism but a declaration that truth is the goal, not victory. In this lesson, we unpack how this verse establishes the first rule of dialectic: the complete abandonment of partisanship (ta'assub).

Guided Path
1
Read the verse in context
Examine Surah Saba verse 24. Notice the phrase 'إنا أو إياكم' – it places both sides on an equal footing in the search for truth. The believers do not claim certainty for themselves; they open the door for mutual correction.
The verse is addressed to polytheists who reject Tawhid.
2
Identify the core attitude
The verse embodies 'غاية التخلي عن التعصب' – the utmost abandonment of partisanship. The speaker is willing to concede that the truth may lie with the opponent. This is the opposite of dogmatic insistence.
This attitude is the first rule of debate (adab al-bahth).
3
Understand the underlying assumption
The author notes that the subject (Tawhid vs. shirk) is a fundamental contradiction: one is truth and the other falsehood. Therefore, the statement does not endorse skepticism but rather a sincere commitment to follow the evidence wherever it leads.
This prevents the rule from being misunderstood as moral relativism.
4
Apply the rule to a contemporary debate
Imagine a debate between two scholars on whether reciting the basmalah aloud in prayer is sunnah or not. Both must be ready to abandon their prior position if the other provides stronger dalil.
This is how the rule operationalizes truth-seeking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صلوات الله عليه والمسلمين في سورة (سبأ ٣٤)، أن يقولوا للمشركين في مناظرتهم لهم : ﴿وَإِنَّا أَوْ إِيَّاكُمْ لَعَلَى هُدًى أَوْ فِي ضَلَالٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ . وفي هذا غاية التخلي عن التعصب لأمر سابق، وكمال إعلان الرغبة بنشدان الحقيقة أنى كانت . ولما كان موضوع المناظرة الذي وردت هذه الآية في صدده توحيد الخالق أو الإشراك به، وهما أمران على طرفي نقيض، لا لقاء بينهما بحال من الأحوال، وهما يدوران حول أصل عظيم من أصول العقيدة الدينية، كان من الأمور البدهية أن الهداية في أحدهما إذ هو الحق، وأن الضلال المبين في الآخر إذ هو الباطل، ومن أجل ذلك كانت عبارة إعلان التخلي عن التعصب لأمر سابق، تتضمن الاعتراف بهذه الحقيقة .
Peace be upon him and the Muslims in Surah Saba (34), that they say to the polytheists in their debate with them: 'Indeed, either we or you are upon guidance or in manifest error.' And in this is the utmost abandonment of partisanship for a prior matter, and complete declaration of the desire to seek truth wherever it is. And since the subject of the debate that this verse was revealed concerning was the oneness of the Creator or associating partners with Him, and they are two matters at opposite extremes, no meeting between them in any state, and they revolve around a great principle of religious creed, it was among the self-evident matters that guidance is in one of them as it is the truth, and manifest error is in the other as it is falsehood. For that reason, the phrase declaring abandonment of partisanship for a prior matter included acknowledgment of this fact.
Source pages p. 368

Commentary
This passage establishes the first rule of dialectical engagement by citing a Qur'anic verse where believers are instructed to address polytheists with a statement that acknowledges the possibility of error on their own side. The verse from Surah Saba (34) is presented as embodying the principle of abandoning partisanship (ta'assub). The author explains that the context of the verse is the fundamental issue of Tawhid versus shirk, which are contradictory. The self-evident truth is that only one side is correct. The verse's wording, which places both parties on equal footing in the search for truth, implicitly requires the debater to set aside prior bias and seek truth wherever it may be. This rule is foundational for objective and fair debate.

Key Takeaway
The first rule of dialectic is the abandonment of prior bias, as modeled by the believers in Surah Saba, ensuring that debate is a sincere search for truth.
