Speech Conduct and Accepting Truth (Adab 8-14)
Etiquettes 8-14 and conclusion on obstinacy and evasion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish proper debate speech conduct from prohibited behaviors like mockery, ambiguity, interruption, and stubbornness.

Core Idea
Discipline in speech and humility in accepting truth protect the debate from degenerating into conflict.

The final set of etiquettes govern how disputants speak and listen: avoid mockery, obscure language, irrelevance, and interruptions; accept truth when it appears. The passage concludes by condemning obstinacy and evasion.

Guided Path
1
List the speech-related etiquettes (8-13)
Identify rules that prohibit mockery (8), excessive brevity or length (9), ambiguous words (10), irrelevance (11), interrupting before understanding (12), and cutting off speech (13).
These ensure clarity and respect.
2
Understand the final etiquette (14) on accepting truth
Rule 14 requires accepting the opponent's truth or acknowledging the strength of his evidence. Note the exceptions: it can be provisional until stronger counterevidence appears.
This is the climax of the adab.
3
Analyze the conclusion on forbidden behavior
The author distinguishes between legitimate acceptance and prohibited obstinacy (mukabara) and evasion (murawagha). What are the consequences of evasion?
Evasion turns debate into pointless combat.

Etiquettes of the disputants according to the scholars of the art of debate and disputation. The scholars of the art of debate and disputation laid down a set of etiquettes that they required disputants to adhere to, safeguarding the soundness of the debate and achieving its purpose. We mention the most important of them below: 1 - That the disputant avoid debating someone of authority whom he fears, lest that affect him and weaken him from presenting his proof as he should. 2 - That the disputant not consider his opponent contemptible, weak, and insignificant, for that reduces his attention, enabling his weak opponent to overcome him. 3 - That he not consider his opponent much stronger than himself, so that he does not become demoralized and fail to present his proof in the required manner. 4 - That he not be in a state of psychological anxiety and disturbance, or in a need that spoils his intellectual and psychological state, such as being hungry, thirsty, needing to urinate, needing to defecate, or the like. 5 - That the two disputants face each other in the gathering, and each see the other if possible, and that they be similar or close in knowledge and stature. 6 - That the disputant not be hasty, intending to silence his opponent in a short time, because that spoils his intellectual composure, distances him from the method of sound logic, and from thinking in reaching the truth. 7 - That each of the disputants intend to contribute to manifesting the truth, even if by the hand of his opponent. 8 - That each of them avoid mockery and ridicule, and everything that suggests contempt and disdain for the opponent, or labeling him with ignorance or lack of understanding, such as smiling, laughing, winking, mocking, and slandering. 9 - That the disputant beware of abbreviating speech in a way that impairs it, and of prolonging speech with no expected benefit. 10 - That the disputant avoid strange words and ambiguous words that admit several meanings, without giving preponderance to the intended one. 11 - That each of the disputants produce speech appropriate to the topic, not straying from what they are discussing. 12 - That neither of them interrupt the speech of his opponent before fully understanding his intent. 13 - That each of them wait for his companion until he finishes his speech, and not cut off his speech before he completes it. 14 - That each of them accept the truth to which his opponent has guided him, or acknowledge that the strength of his evidence gives preponderance to his viewpoint or his school, until something else is discovered that weakens his evidence and renders it unsuitable for preponderance. As for insistence on rejection, it is obstinacy that is prohibited. And as for evasion, it is escape and withdrawal from the debating session. Whenever a disputant finds this evasion from his opponent, it is better for him to cut off the debate and force his opponent to flee and withdraw. And let him beware of being drawn into another topic, then another, and so on, so that the debate turns into something resembling free wrestling that has no constraints or regulations. And this is forbidden argument.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a practical guide from the Islamic dialectical tradition, laying down fourteen etiquettes (adab) for disputants in formal debate (munazara). The author begins by stating that scholars of the art of debate established these rules to preserve the integrity of debate and achieve its true purpose: the discovery of truth. The fourteen points are then enumerated sequentially. The first three deal with the disputant's perception of his opponent: he must neither fear a powerful opponent (1), nor despise a weak one (2), nor overestimate his opponent's strength (3). These ensure balanced engagement. The fourth addresses physical and psychological states: one must not debate while hungry, thirsty, or needing to relieve oneself, as these impair focus. The fifth emphasizes physical arrangement and parity in knowledge. The sixth warns against haste aimed at silencing the opponent quickly. The seventh is pivotal: each disputant should seek to manifest the truth even if it comes from the opponent. The eighth prohibits mockery and contemptuous gestures. The ninth advises against overly terse or excessively long speech. The tenth cautions against obscure or ambiguous language. The eleventh requires relevance to the topic. The twelfth and thirteenth demand patience: one must understand the opponent's argument before responding, and wait for him to finish speaking. The fourteenth is the climax: the disputant must accept the truth when it becomes evident, or at least acknowledge the strength of the opponent's evidence until a stronger counterargument appears. The passage concludes by condemning stubborn refusal (mukabara) and evasion (murawagha), warning that evasive tactics degrade the debate into unconstrained wrestling. This whole passage exemplifies the ethical framework of Islamic jadal, prioritizing truth over victory.

Key Terms
مكابرة
مراوغة
حجة

Eight Ethical Rules of Debate Conduct
Author enumerates fourteen ethical rules for debate conduct (here rules 7-14).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the eight ethical rules of debate (munazara) from the text, and recognize prohibited behaviors like obstinacy (mukābara) and evasion (murāwaghā).

Core Idea
Truth is the goal; victory is not. Debate rules safeguard the search for truth.

This passage lays down eight essential rules for ethical debate (munazara). These rules are not mere courtesies; they are the structural safeguards that ensure debate remains a tool for discovering truth, not a battlefield for egos. Understanding these rules equips you to participate in or evaluate dialectical exchanges in Islamic sciences — from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching goal (Rule 7)
Read Rule 7: Each disputant should aim to manifest truth, even if through the opponent. This is the foundation of adab al-munazara.
2
Forbid mockery and contempt (Rule 8)
Notice the list of forbidden behaviors: laughing, winking, etc. All demean the opponent and corrupt the debate.
3
Regulate speech clarity (Rules 9-10)
Avoid overly brief or excessively long speech. Also avoid ambiguous words. The goal is clear communication.
4
Maintain relevance and patience (Rules 11-13)
Stick to the topic, understand the opponent's point before responding, and do not interrupt.
5
Accept truth or acknowledge stronger evidence (Rule 14)
Rule 14 demands intellectual honesty: accept truth or concede the strength of evidence. The text then warns against obstinacy and evasion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

۸ - أن يجتنب كلُّ منهما الهزء والسخرية، وكل ما يشعر باحتقار المناظر وازدرائه لصاحبه، أو وسمه بالجهل أو قلة الفهم، كالتبسم والضحك والغمز والهمز واللمز .

۱۰ - أن يجتنب المناظر الألفاظ الغريبة، والألفاظ المجملة التي تحتمل عدة معان، من غير ترجيح أحدها الذي هو المراد .
۱۱ - أن يأتي كل من المتناظرين بالكلام الملائم للموضوع، فلا يخرج عما هما بصدده .
۱۲ - ألا يتعرض أحدهما لكلام خصمه قبل أن يفهم مراده تماماً .
۱۳ - أن ينتظر كل واحد منهما صاحبه حتى يفرغ من كلامه، ولا يقطع عليه كلامه قبل أن يتمه .
١٤ - أن يقبل كلُّ منهما الحقَّ الذي هداه إليه مُناظره، أو يعترف بأنّ قوة دليله تُقدّم ترجيحاً لوجهة نظره، أو لمذهبه، حتى يُكتشف شيء آخر يُضعفُ دليله، ويجعله غير صالح للترجيح .
أما الإصرار على الرفض فمكابرة ممنوعة، وأما المراوغة فهي تهرب وانسحاب من مجلس المناظرة. ومتى وجد المناظر هذه المراوغة من خصمه فمن الخير له أن يقطع المناظرة، ويُلزم خصمه بالهروب والانسحاب. وليحذر من أن يستدرجه إلى موضوع آخر، ثم آخر، وهكذا، فتتحوّل المناظرة إلى ما يشبه المصارعة الحرّة التي ليس لها قيود ولا ضوابط . وهذا جدال محظور .
7. Each of the two disputants should aim to contribute to manifesting the truth, even if it be at the hand of his opponent. 8. Each of them should avoid mockery and sarcasm, and everything that suggests contempt and disdain of the disputant for his companion, or branding him with ignorance or lack of understanding, such as smiling, laughing, winking, hinting, and backbiting. 9. The disputant should be cautious of abbreviating speech in a way that disrupts meaning, and of prolonging speech with no hoped-for benefit. 10. The disputant should avoid strange words and vague words that admit several meanings, without specifying one of them as intended. 11. Each of the two disputants should utter speech appropriate to the subject, not straying from what they are addressing. 12. Neither should engage with his opponent's speech before fully understanding his intent. 13. Each should wait for his companion until he finishes his speech, and not interrupt him before he completes it. 14. Each should accept the truth to which his disputant guides him, or acknowledge that the strength of his evidence gives preference to his viewpoint or his school, until something else is discovered that weakens his evidence and renders it unsuitable for preference. As for insistence on rejection, it is forbidden obstinacy. As for evasion, it is flight and withdrawal from the debate session. When a disputant encounters this evasion from his opponent, it is better for him to terminate the debate and oblige his opponent to flee and withdraw. And he should beware of being lured into another topic, then another, and so on, so that the debate turns into something resembling a free-for-all wrestle with no restraints or controls. This is forbidden argumentation.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage enumerates fourteen rules (points 7–14, continuing from a previous discussion) of ethical conduct in dialectical debate (munazara). The author first emphasizes that the primary goal of each disputant should be to help manifest the truth, even if it appears through the opponent's argument. Then he forbids mockery and any behavior that belittles the other, such as smiling or gesturing disdainfully. Next, he advises against speech that is excessively brief (thus unclear) or unnecessarily lengthy. He also warns against using ambiguous or obscure words. He then requires relevance: each must stick to the topic. He stresses the importance of understanding the opponent's speech before responding, and not interrupting. The final rule is intellectual honesty: accepting the truth when it becomes clear, or at least acknowledging the strength of the opponent's evidence, while remaining open to future counter-evidence. The author then comments on two negative behaviors: insistence on rejection (obstinacy) and evasion (changing the subject or withdrawing). He advises the disputant to end the debate if the opponent resorts to evasion, and not to be drawn into a spiral of topic shifts that turn the debate into a chaotic free-for-all. This passage is a classic example of adab al-munazara, emphasizing that the purpose of debate is not victory but truth.

Two Pillars of Debate and Roles of Participants
Author defines the two essential pillars of debate and the roles of participants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two pillars of debate and correctly assign the roles of mustadill, mani', sa'il, and mu'allil to participants based on the type of subject under discussion.

Core Idea
Debate needs two sides and a topic; roles shift with the subject type.

In this passage, the author lays out the fundamental structure of any dialectical exchange. You will learn that debate (munazara) requires two essential pillars: a subject (mas'ala) and two teams. Moreover, the roles of the objector and proponent change depending on whether the subject is a definition/division or a proposition (tasdiq). A clarifying footnote ensures we don't confuse the 'subject' of debate with the logical subject term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pillars
The passage states debate has two essential pillars: a subject (mawḍūʿ) and two teams. The subject is the issue under discussion, not a logical term.
Memorise this structure before moving to roles.
2
Distinguish subject types
The author divides subjects into two categories: definition/division (taʿrīf/taqsīm) and proposition (tasdīq). The category determines the terminology for roles.
Propositions can be explicit or implicit.
3
Define the roles for definition subjects
If the subject is a definition or division, the objector is called mustadill (he adduces evidence against it), and the proponent is called māniʿ (he defends the definition).
4
Define the roles for proposition subjects
If the subject is a proposition (tasdīq), the objector is called sāʾil (questioner) and the proponent is called muʿallil (he gives reasons).
5
Understand role dynamics
Roles can swap: if the sāʾil introduces a new proposition, he becomes muʿallil and the other becomes sāʾil. This shows debate is dynamic.
This prevents a false assumption that roles are fixed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَرْكَانُ المُنَاظَرَةِ
للمناظرة ركنان أساسيان هما:
الركن الأول: موضوع تجري حوله المناظرة.
الركن الثاني: فريقان يتحاوران حول موضوع المناظرة، أحدهما: مدّعٍ أو ناقل خبر، والآخر: معترض عليه.
فإن كان الموضوع تعريفاً أو تقسيماً، سمي المعترض عليه (مستدلاً)، وسمي صاحب التعريف أو التقسيم (مانعاً).
وإن كان الموضوع (تصديقاً) - أي قضية منطقية سواء أكانت مصرّحاً بها أو مفهومة من ضمن الكلام - فالمعترض عليه يسمى (سائلاً)، وصاحب التصديق ومقدّمه يسمى (مُعلّلاً).
ويكون (المعلّل) في الأصل هو البادىء بالكلام، ثم يوجه (السائل) عليه اعتراضه. وقد ينعكس الأمر ضمن المناظرة، وذلك إذ يتحول (السائل) إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد، فيكون حينئذٍ (معلّلاً)، ويكون مقابله الذي يعترض عليه (سائلاً)، وهكذا كلّما تحوّل السائل إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد.
شروط المناظرة:
يشترط في المناظرة أربعة شروط: (١) المراد بالموضوع المسألة أو نقطة البحث، لا (الموضوع) المنطقي الذي يقابله المحمول.
The pillars of debate: Debate has two essential pillars: The first pillar: a subject around which the debate revolves. The second pillar: two teams that discuss the subject of the debate; one of them is a claimant or transmitter of a report, and the other is an objector to him. If the subject is a definition or a division, the objector is called 'mustadill' (one who adduces evidence), and the owner of the definition or division is called 'mani' (preventer/contradictor). And if the subject is a 'tasdiq' (assent) — i.e., a logical proposition, whether explicitly stated or understood from the speech — then the objector is called 'sa'il' (questioner), and the owner of the assent and its advocate is called 'mu'allil' (one who gives a reason). The mu'allil is originally the one who starts the speech, then the sa'il directs his objection against him. The situation may reverse within the debate, when the sa'il becomes the presenter of a new assent, then he becomes a mu'allil, and his counterpart who objects becomes a sa'il, and so on whenever the sa'il turns into a presenter of a new assent. Conditions of debate: There are four conditions required in debate: (1) By 'subject' is meant the issue or point of research, not the logical subject that is contrasted with the predicate.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines the two pillars (arkān) of debate (munāẓara). The first pillar is the subject (mawḍūʿ) under discussion, which the footnote clarifies is the issue or point of research, not the logical subject term opposed to the predicate. The second pillar is the two teams: one is a claimant (muddaʿī) or transmitter of a report (nāqil khabar), the other is an objector (muʿtariḍ). The author then distinguishes two cases based on the type of subject. If the subject is a definition (taʿrīf) or a division (taqsīm), the objector is called mustadill (one who adduces evidence to challenge the definition or division), and the one who proposed the definition or division is called māniʿ (preventer, because he defends it). If the subject is a tasdīq (assent), i.e., a proposition (whether explicitly stated or implicit), then the objector is called sāʾil (questioner) and the presenter of the proposition is called muʿallil (one who provides the reason/evidence). The muʿallil is the one who initiates the speech, and the sāʾil then raises an objection. However, roles can swap during the debate: if the sāʾil introduces a new proposition, he becomes the muʿallil, and the other becomes the sāʾil. This describes the dynamic structure of a dialectical exchange. Finally, the passage begins to list conditions of debate, stating there are four, but only the first is given: the clarification of 'subject' as the issue under discussion.

Key Terms
الركن
المستدل
المانع
السائل
المعلل

Key Takeaway
The roles in Islamic debate are defined by the type of subject and can dynamically shift, with the sāʾil potentially becoming a muʿallil if he advances a new proposition.

The Two Pillars of Formal Debate
Author defines the two pillars and distinguishes role names by topic type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two pillars of munāẓarah and classify the roles of debaters based on whether the topic is a definition/division or an assertion.

Core Idea
Every formal debate rests on a topic and two parties with defined roles.

Every structured debate, whether in theology or jurisprudence, has an essential skeleton: a subject being debated and two sides arguing it. This lesson unpacks those two pillars and shows how the roles of the debaters shift depending on what kind of claim is under discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pillars
The author states that debate has two pillars (ركنان): a topic (موضوع) and two groups (فريقان). Look for these in the Arabic text.
2
Classify the topic type
The topic can be a definition (تعريف) or division (تقسيم), or it can be an assertion (تصديق). Note how the passage distinguishes these.
An assertion is a logical proposition that is claimed to be true.
3
Map the roles based on topic type
When the topic is a definition/division, the objector is called mustadill (مستدل) and the proponent is called māniʿ (مانع). When the topic is an assertion, the objector is sāʾil (سائل) and the proponent is muʿallil (معلل). Determine which role you would have in a given debate scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَرْكَانُ المُنَاظَرَةِ
للمناظرة ركنان أساسيان هما:
الركن الأول: موضوع تجري حوله المناظرة.
الركن الثاني: فريقان يتحاوران حول موضوع المناظرة، أحدهما: مدّعٍ أو ناقل خبر، والآخر: معترض عليه.
فإن كان الموضوع تعريفاً أو تقسيماً، سمي المعترض عليه (مستدلاً)، وسمي صاحب التعريف أو التقسيم (مانعاً).
وإن كان الموضوع (تصديقاً) - أي قضية منطقية سواء أكانت مصرّحاً بها أو مفهومة من ضمن الكلام - فالمعترض عليه يسمى (سائلاً)، وصاحب التصديق ومقدّمه يسمى (مُعلّلاً).
ويكون (المعلّل) في الأصل هو البادىء بالكلام، ثم يوجه (السائل) عليه اعتراضه. وقد ينعكس الأمر ضمن المناظرة، وذلك إذ يتحول (السائل) إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد، فيكون حينئذٍ (معلّلاً)، ويكون مقابله الذي يعترض عليه (سائلاً)، وهكذا كلّما تحوّل السائل إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد.
شروط المناظرة:
يشترط في المناظرة أربعة شروط:
الشرط الأول: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بما يحتاج إليه من قوانين المناظرة وقواعدها، حول الموضوع الذي يريدان المناظرة فيه.
الشرط الثاني: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بالموضوع الذي يتنازعان فيه، حتى يتكلم كل منهما ضمن الوظيفة المأذون له بها في قواعد المناظرة وضوابطها، فإذا تكلم لم يخبط خبط عشواء، ولم يناقش في البدهيات بغير علم، وإذا ألزم بالحق التزم به دون مكابرة.
الشرط الثالث: أن يكون الموضوع مما يجوز أن تجري فيه المناظرة ضمن قواعد هذا الفن وضوابطه. فالمفردات والبديهيات الجلية مثلاً، لا تجري فيها المناظرة أصلاً، كما سيأتي بيانه، لذلك فلا يصح أن يكون موضوع المناظرة من هذا القبيل.
الشرط الرابع: أن يُجري المتناظران مناظرتهما على عرف واحد، فإذا كان كلام (المعلّل) جارياً مثلاً على عرف الفقهاء، فليس (للسائل) العارف بذلك، أن يعترض عليه استناداً إلى عرف النحاة، أو الوضع اللغوي، أو عرف الفلاسفة، أو نحو ذلك.
Pillars of the Debate
For the debate there are two essential pillars:
The first pillar: a topic around which the debate takes place.
The second pillar: two groups who converse around the topic of the debate, one of them: a claimant or news-transmitter, and the other: an objector against him.
If the topic is a definition or a division, the one objected to is called (mustadill), and the one who owns the definition or division is called (māniʿ).
And if the topic is (tasdīq) - i.e. a logical proposition, whether explicitly stated or understood from the speech - then the one objected to is called (sāʾil), and the one who owns the tasdīq and its introduction is called (muʿallil).
And the (muʿallil) is originally the one who begins speaking, then the (sāʾil) directs his objection against him. And the matter may be reversed within the debate, that is when the (sāʾil) becomes a proponent of a new tasdīq, then at that point he is (muʿallil), and his counterpart who objects to him becomes (sāʾil), and so on whenever the sāʾil becomes a proponent of a new tasdīq.
Conditions of the Debate:
Four conditions are required in the debate:
The first condition: that the two debaters have knowledge of what is needed of the rules and principles of debate, concerning the topic they wish to debate about.
The second condition: that the two debaters have knowledge of the topic they are disputing, so that each speaks within the function permitted to him in the rules and regulations of debate. So when he speaks, he does not flounder blindly, nor does he discuss self-evident matters without knowledge, and if he is compelled to the truth, he adheres to it without obstinacy.
The third condition: that the topic be of the kind which it is permissible to conduct debate within the rules of this art and its regulations. Simple terms and clear self-evident matters, for example, are not subject to debate at all, as will be explained later; therefore it is not valid for the topic of debate to be of this sort.
The fourth condition: that the two debaters conduct their debate on a single convention. So if, for example, the speech of the (muʿallil) follows the convention of the jurists, it is not permissible for the (sāʾil) who knows that to object against him based on the convention of the grammarians, or the lexical usage, or the convention of the philosophers, or the like.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines the two essential pillars and four conditions of munāẓarah (debate) as a formal logical discipline. The author first states that debate rests on two pillars: a topic and two parties. The parties are a claimant/news-transmitter and an objector. The roles are named differently depending on the type of topic. If the topic is a definition or division, the objector is called mustadill (one who provides evidence) and the proponent is called māniʿ (one who prevents). If the topic is a tasdīq (assertion/proposition), the objector is called sāʾil (questioner) and the proponent is called muʿallil (one who provides a cause). The author then explains that the muʿallil begins the speech, and the sāʾil objects, but roles can reverse if the sāʾil advances a new assertion. This dynamic ensures a structured exchange. The passage then lists four conditions: (1) both debaters must know the rules of debate; (2) both must know the topic well, speaking within their permitted function, avoid blind argumentation, and accept truth without obstinacy; (3) the topic must be such that debate is permissible – simple terms and clear self-evident truths are excluded; (4) both debaters must adhere to a single convention (ʿurf), e.g., if one speaks according to juristic usage, the other cannot object based on grammatical or philosophical usage. The footnote clarifies that 'topic' here means the issue under discussion, not the logical subject. The passage establishes the foundational procedure for formal dialectic.

In a debate about a definition (taʿrīf), what is the term for the person being objected to?
A) سَائِل (sāʾil)
B) مَانِع (māniʿ)
C) مُسْتَدِلّ (mustadill)
D) مُعَلِّل (muʿallil)

Key Takeaway
Every formal debate has two pillars: a topic and two parties, with role names determined by whether the topic is a definition/division or an assertion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate about whether analogy (qiyas) is a valid source of law in Usul al-Fiqh, identify the topic type and the roles: is it a definition (of qiyas) or an assertion (that qiyas is valid)? Then assign the correct terms (mustadill/māniʿ or sāʾil/muʿallil) to each debater.

The Four Conditions of Munāẓarah
Author lists and explains the four conditions of valid debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four conditions for a valid formal debate and evaluate a given debate scenario for compliance with these conditions.

Core Idea
A valid debate requires knowledge of rules, knowledge of the topic, debatability of the topic, and a shared convention.

Not every heated discussion qualifies as a formal munāẓarah. The author lays down four conditions that transform a dispute into a disciplined search for truth. This lesson walks through each condition and its rationale, using examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Extract the four conditions
The passage lists four conditions (شروط) for debate. Find them in the Arabic text: knowledge of debate rules, knowledge of the topic, permissible topic, and shared convention (ʿurf).
2
Understand each condition's purpose
For each condition, ask: why is it necessary? Condition 1 prevents procedural chaos; Condition 2 ensures informed argument; Condition 3 excludes self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt); Condition 4 prevents cross-discipline confusion.
For condition 3, simple terms and clear self-evident matters are not debatable because they are already certain.
3
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Imagine a debate between a jurist and a grammarian about the permissibility of a contract. The jurist uses fiqh terminology. According to condition 4, the grammarian cannot object based on linguistic definitions if both agreed to the fiqh convention. Determine whether the debate is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَرْكَانُ المُنَاظَرَةِ
للمناظرة ركنان أساسيان هما:
الركن الأول: موضوع تجري حوله المناظرة.
الركن الثاني: فريقان يتحاوران حول موضوع المناظرة، أحدهما: مدّعٍ أو ناقل خبر، والآخر: معترض عليه.
فإن كان الموضوع تعريفاً أو تقسيماً، سمي المعترض عليه (مستدلاً)، وسمي صاحب التعريف أو التقسيم (مانعاً).
وإن كان الموضوع (تصديقاً) - أي قضية منطقية سواء أكانت مصرّحاً بها أو مفهومة من ضمن الكلام - فالمعترض عليه يسمى (سائلاً)، وصاحب التصديق ومقدّمه يسمى (مُعلّلاً).
ويكون (المعلّل) في الأصل هو البادىء بالكلام، ثم يوجه (السائل) عليه اعتراضه. وقد ينعكس الأمر ضمن المناظرة، وذلك إذ يتحول (السائل) إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد، فيكون حينئذٍ (معلّلاً)، ويكون مقابله الذي يعترض عليه (سائلاً)، وهكذا كلّما تحوّل السائل إلى مقدّم تصديق جديد.
شروط المناظرة:
يشترط في المناظرة أربعة شروط:
الشرط الأول: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بما يحتاج إليه من قوانين المناظرة وقواعدها، حول الموضوع الذي يريدان المناظرة فيه.
الشرط الثاني: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بالموضوع الذي يتنازعان فيه، حتى يتكلم كل منهما ضمن الوظيفة المأذون له بها في قواعد المناظرة وضوابطها، فإذا تكلم لم يخبط خبط عشواء، ولم يناقش في البدهيات بغير علم، وإذا ألزم بالحق التزم به دون مكابرة.
الشرط الثالث: أن يكون الموضوع مما يجوز أن تجري فيه المناظرة ضمن قواعد هذا الفن وضوابطه. فالمفردات والبديهيات الجلية مثلاً، لا تجري فيها المناظرة أصلاً، كما سيأتي بيانه، لذلك فلا يصح أن يكون موضوع المناظرة من هذا القبيل.
الشرط الرابع: أن يُجري المتناظران مناظرتهما على عرف واحد، فإذا كان كلام (المعلّل) جارياً مثلاً على عرف الفقهاء، فليس (للسائل) العارف بذلك، أن يعترض عليه استناداً إلى عرف النحاة، أو الوضع اللغوي، أو عرف الفلاسفة، أو نحو ذلك.
Pillars of the Debate
For the debate there are two essential pillars:
The first pillar: a topic around which the debate takes place.
The second pillar: two groups who converse around the topic of the debate, one of them: a claimant or news-transmitter, and the other: an objector against him.
If the topic is a definition or a division, the one objected to is called (mustadill), and the one who owns the definition or division is called (māniʿ).
And if the topic is (tasdīq) - i.e. a logical proposition, whether explicitly stated or understood from the speech - then the one objected to is called (sāʾil), and the one who owns the tasdīq and its introduction is called (muʿallil).
And the (muʿallil) is originally the one who begins speaking, then the (sāʾil) directs his objection against him. And the matter may be reversed within the debate, that is when the (sāʾil) becomes a proponent of a new tasdīq, then at that point he is (muʿallil), and his counterpart who objects to him becomes (sāʾil), and so on whenever the sāʾil becomes a proponent of a new tasdīq.
Conditions of the Debate:
Four conditions are required in the debate:
The first condition: that the two debaters have knowledge of what is needed of the rules and principles of debate, concerning the topic they wish to debate about.
The second condition: that the two debaters have knowledge of the topic they are disputing, so that each speaks within the function permitted to him in the rules and regulations of debate. So when he speaks, he does not flounder blindly, nor does he discuss self-evident matters without knowledge, and if he is compelled to the truth, he adheres to it without obstinacy.
The third condition: that the topic be of the kind which it is permissible to conduct debate within the rules of this art and its regulations. Simple terms and clear self-evident matters, for example, are not subject to debate at all, as will be explained later; therefore it is not valid for the topic of debate to be of this sort.
The fourth condition: that the two debaters conduct their debate on a single convention. So if, for example, the speech of the (muʿallil) follows the convention of the jurists, it is not permissible for the (sāʾil) who knows that to object against him based on the convention of the grammarians, or the lexical usage, or the convention of the philosophers, or the like.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage defines the two essential pillars and four conditions of munāẓarah (debate) as a formal logical discipline. The author first states that debate rests on two pillars: a topic and two parties. The parties are a claimant/news-transmitter and an objector. The roles are named differently depending on the type of topic. Checkpoint
According to the passage, why should simple terms and clear self-evident matters not be the topic of debate?
A) Because they are too difficult to argue about
B) Because they are already certain and do not admit of debate
C) Because the rules of debate forbid it
D) Because they are not part of any convention

Key Takeaway
A valid formal debate requires that both parties know the rules and the topic, that the topic is debatable, and that they share a single disciplinary convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate between a theologian (mutakallim) and a philosopher (faylasūf) about whether the world is eternal. The theologian argues using Kalam conventions, while the philosopher objects using Aristotelian physics. Does this satisfy the fourth condition? If not, what would need to change?

Role Reversal in Munāẓarah
Author explains dynamic role reversal when the objector proposes a new assertion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe how roles can reverse when the objector advances a new assertion, and explain why this is essential for dynamic debate.

Core Idea
In formal debate, roles are fluid: the objector can become the proponent by introducing a new claim.

A debate is not a static ping-pong match. The author describes a crucial feature: the person objecting (sāʾil) can, in turn, advance a new assertion and become the muʿallil, while the former proponent becomes the sāʾil. This role reversal keeps the debate alive and allows for multiple layers of argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reversal mechanism
The passage says: 'when the sāʾil becomes a proponent of a new tasdīq, then he is muʿallil, and his counterpart who objects to him becomes sāʾil.' Find this in the Arabic.
2
Understand why reversal occurs
Reversal happens when the objector, instead of merely rejecting the proponent's claim, puts forward a counter-assertion. This shifts the burden of proof back to the original objector.
3
Apply reversal to a historical debate
In a debate about divine attributes, the Muʿtazilī (muʿallil) asserts that God's attributes are identical to His essence. The Ashʿarī (sāʾil) objects. If the Ashʿarī then asserts that attributes are distinct, he becomes the muʿallil for that new assertion, and the Muʿtazilī becomes the sāʾil. Trace this reversal.

The First Two Conditions: Knowledge of Rules and Subject
Author enumerates four prerequisites for debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why both debaters must know the rules of debate and the subject matter, and identify violations of these conditions.

Core Idea
Debate requires prior knowledge of both the rules and the subject.

The first two conditions of a valid debate focus on the epistemological preparedness of the participants. Without shared knowledge of the rules and the subject, a debate degenerates into random argument. This lesson breaks down these two foundational prerequisites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition in the Arabic
Locate the sentence beginning with 'الشرط الأول'. Read it carefully. It states that the two debaters must have knowledge of the rules of debate needed for the subject.
2
Identify the second condition in the Arabic
Find the sentence starting with 'الشرط الثاني'. It requires knowledge of the subject being disputed, and adds that the debater must speak only within his allowed role, avoid random errors, and accept truth when obligated.
This condition also prohibits discussing self-evident truths ignorantly, which ties to condition three.
3
Explain the rationale for both conditions
The rules of debate provide a structured framework; ignorance leads to chaos. Knowledge of the subject ensures arguments are relevant and grounded. Together, they prevent empty or misleading discourse.
Without these, the debate lacks epistemological integrity.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Imagine a debate about the permissibility of qiyas in usul al-fiqh. If one debater does not know the rules of qiyas (condition 1) or the details of the specific case (condition 2), the debate would be invalid. Explain why.
This applies the conditions to a concrete Islamic science example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الأول: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بما يحتاج إليه من قوانين المناظرة وقواعدها، حول الموضوع الذي يريدان المناظرة فيه.
الشرط الثاني: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بالموضوع الذي يتنازعان فيه، حتى يتكلم كل منهما ضمن الوظيفة المأذون له بها في قواعد المناظرة وضوابطها، فإذا تكلم لم يخبط خبط عشواء، ولم يناقش في البدهيات بغير علم، وإذا ألزم بالحق التزم به دون مكابرة.
الشرط الثالث: أن يكون الموضوع مما يجوز أن تجري فيه المناظرة ضمن قواعد هذا الفن وضوابطه.
فالمفردات والبديهيات الجلية مثلاً، لا تجري فيها المناظرة أصلاً، كما سيأتي بيانه، لذلك فلا يصح أن يكون موضوع المناظرة من هذا القبيل.
الشرط الرابع: أن يُجري المتناظران مناظرتهما على عرف واحد، فإذا كان كلام (المعلّل) جارياً مثلاً على عرف الفقهاء، فليس (للسائل) العارف بذلك، أن يعترض عليه استناداً إلى عرف النحاة، أو الوضع اللغوي، أو عرف الفلاسفة، أو نحو ذلك.
The first condition: The two debaters should have knowledge of the rules and principles of debate that are needed regarding the subject they wish to debate. The second condition: The two debaters should have knowledge of the subject they are disputing, so that each speaks within the function permitted to him by the rules and regulations of debate. If he speaks, he does not blunder randomly, and he does not discuss self-evident truths without knowledge. If he is obligated to the truth, he adheres to it without stubbornness. The third condition: The subject should be such that debate is permissible within the rules and regulations of this art. For example, simple terms and clear self-evident truths are not subject to debate at all, as will be explained, so it is not valid for the subject of debate to be of this kind. The fourth condition: The two debaters should conduct their debate according to a single convention. For example, if the proponent's speech follows the convention of the jurists, then the objector who knows that should not object based on the convention of grammarians, or linguistic usage, or the convention of philosophers, or similar.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage enumerates four essential conditions for a valid debate (munazara). The first condition requires that both parties be knowledgeable in the rules of debate itself. The second condition requires knowledge of the specific subject under dispute, speaking only within their allowed role, avoiding random errors and acknowledging truth. The third condition specifies that the subject must be debatable; self-evident truths (badihiyyat) are not fit for debate. The fourth condition emphasizes a common framework: if the proponent (mu'allil) uses juristic convention, the objector (sa'il) cannot object based on a different convention like grammar or philosophy.

Key Terms
المتناظران
المعلّل
السائل
المناظرة
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first condition, what must the two debaters have knowledge of?
A) The rules of debate only
B) The subject matter only
C) Both the rules of debate and the subject matter
D) The personal opinions of the opponents

Key Takeaway
A valid debate requires both procedural and substantive knowledge from all participants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a scenario where condition 1 is violated: a debater in a discussion about the binding nature of ijma' does not know the rules of how ijma' is established. Explain why the debate would be invalid.
?
Identify a debate topic from Islamic theology that might violate condition 2 if one debater does not know the subject. For example, debating the attributes of Allah without knowledge of Ash'ari or Maturidi doctrines.

The Last Two Conditions: Permissible Topic and Shared Convention
Author enumerates four prerequisites for debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given topic is permissible for debate and identify the convention being used.

Core Idea
Debate is only possible on non-self-evident topics and within a shared framework.

The third and fourth conditions of debate focus on the subject and the framework. A debate must be about a topic that is genuinely disputable—not a self-evident truth—and both sides must operate within the same convention. This lesson examines these two essential constraints.

Guided Path
1
Find the third condition in the Arabic
Locate 'الشرط الثالث' in the Arabic. It states that the subject must be such that debate is permissible within the rules of the art.
2
Identify what topics are excluded
The author gives examples: simple terms (mufradat) and clear self-evident truths (badihiyyat jaliyya) are not debatable. Why? Because they are already known with certainty and cannot be genuinely contested.
This prevents wasting time on unproductive arguments.
3
Find the fourth condition in the Arabic
Locate 'الشرط الرابع'. It requires that the debate be conducted according to a single convention (urf wahid).
4
Explain the consequence of violating condition 4
If the proponent (mu'allil) uses the convention of jurists, the objector (sa'il) cannot object based on grammar, linguistic usage, or philosophy. This prevents equivocation and ensures a level playing field.
The same principle applies in interdisciplinary discussions: each discipline has its own conventions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الأول: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بما يحتاج إليه من قوانين المناظرة وقواعدها، حول الموضوع الذي يريدان المناظرة فيه.
الشرط الثاني: أن يكون المتناظران على معرفة بالموضوع الذي يتنازعان فيه، حتى يتكلم كل منهما ضمن الوظيفة المأذون له بها في قواعد المناظرة وضوابطها، فإذا تكلم لم يخبط خبط عشواء، ولم يناقش في البدهيات بغير علم، وإذا ألزم بالحق التزم به دون مكابرة.
الشرط الثالث: أن يكون الموضوع مما يجوز أن تجري فيه المناظرة ضمن قواعد هذا الفن وضوابطه.
فالمفردات والبديهيات الجلية مثلاً، لا تجري فيها المناظرة أصلاً، كما سيأتي بيانه، لذلك فلا يصح أن يكون موضوع المناظرة من هذا القبيل.
الشرط الرابع: أن يُجري المتناظران مناظرتهما على عرف واحد، فإذا كان كلام (المعلّل) جارياً مثلاً على عرف الفقهاء، فليس (للسائل) العارف بذلك، أن يعترض عليه استناداً إلى عرف النحاة، أو الوضع اللغوي، أو عرف الفلاسفة، أو نحو ذلك.
The first condition: The two debaters should have knowledge of the rules and principles of debate that are needed regarding the subject they wish to debate. The second condition: The two debaters should have knowledge of the subject they are disputing, so that each speaks within the function permitted to him by the rules and regulations of debate. If he speaks, he does not blunder randomly, and he does not discuss self-evident truths without knowledge. If he is obligated to the truth, he adheres to it without stubbornness. The third condition: The subject should be such that debate is permissible within the rules and regulations of this art. For example, simple terms and clear self-evident truths are not subject to debate at all, as will be explained, so it is not valid for the subject of debate to be of this kind. The fourth condition: The two debaters should conduct their debate according to a single convention. For example, if the proponent's speech follows the convention of the jurists, then the objector who knows that should not object based on the convention of grammarians, or linguistic usage, or the convention of philosophers, or similar.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage enumerates four essential conditions for a valid debate (munazara). The first condition requires that both parties be knowledgeable in the rules of debate itself. The second condition requires knowledge of the specific subject under dispute, speaking only within their allowed role, avoiding random errors and acknowledging truth. The third condition specifies that the subject must be debatable; self-evident truths (badihiyyat) are not fit for debate. The fourth condition emphasizes a common framework: if the proponent (mu'allil) uses juristic convention, the objector (sa'il) cannot object based on a different convention like grammar or philosophy.

Key Takeaway
A valid debate must concern a disputable topic and operate within a shared disciplinary framework.

Stages and Outcomes of a Debate
Author outlines the three stages of a valid debate and defines its possible outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three stages of a valid Islamic debate and distinguish between the outcomes of mufham and mulzam.

Core Idea
A debate has a precise logical structure: define the issue, present proofs, conclude with certainty or concession.

This passage from the Islamic dialectical tradition lays out the essential roadmap of a valid munazara. Understanding these stages and outcomes is crucial for participating in or analyzing scholarly disputations. Let's break down the three phases and the two possible verdicts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three stages
List the three named stages in order: al-mabādi’ (principles), al-awāsiṭ (intermediaries), al-maqāṭi‘ (conclusions). Note the purpose of each.
2
Understand the purpose of each stage
The first stage defines the subject of dispute. The second presents proofs (dalā’il) that show the necessity of the claim. The third is termination: either by reaching necessary certainty (ḍarūrī) or a probable conclusion accepted by the opponent.
3
Determine the outcome for each participant
If the proponent (mu‘allil) cannot answer an objection, he is mufham (confounded). If the objector (sā’il) cannot maintain his objection, he is mulzam (cornered).
4
Apply to a real example
Consider a debate about whether the world is created. The stage of principles would define 'world' and 'created'. The intermediaries would present proofs. The conclusion might be necessary (if opponent concedes) or probable. If the proponent fails to rebut an objection, he is mufham; if the objector fails, he is mulzam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥)
مَرَاحِلُ المُنَاظَرَةِ وَنَتِيجَتُهَا
أ - مراحل المناظرة :
تنقسم كل مناظرة سليمة إلى ثلاث مراحل :
المرحلة الأولى : مرحلة (المبادىء)، وفي هذه المرحلة يتم تعيين محل النزاع، حتى لا يتشتت الفريقان في أطراف غير متطابقة، وحتى لا يتكلم كل منهما في وادٍ غير الوادي الذي يتكلم فيه مناظره .
المرحلة الثانية : مرحلة (الأواسط)، وفي هذه المرحلة تُقدَّمُ الدلائل التي يظهر فيها لزوم المطلوب .
المرحلة الثالثة : مرحلة (المقاطع)، وهي مرحلة إذا انتهى البحث إليها انقطع، وهو ينقطع إذا انتهى إلى الضروري (وهو اليقين الذي يجب التسليم به بالضرورة العقلية)، أو إذا انتهى إلى الظني الذي يُسَلَّم به الخصم .
ب - نتيجة المناظرة :
إذا عجز (المعلّل) عن رد اعتراض (السائل)، كان المعلّل (مُفْحَماً).
وإذا عجز (السائل) عن تصحيح اعتراضه كان (مُلْزَماً).
(5) Stages of the debate and its result. A - Stages of the debate: Every valid debate is divided into three stages. The first stage: the stage of principles. In this stage, the subject of dispute is determined so that the two parties do not scatter into mismatched parts, and so that each does not speak in a valley other than the valley his opponent is speaking in. The second stage: the stage of intermediaries. In this stage, proofs are presented in which the necessity of the required conclusion appears. The third stage: the stage of conclusions. This is the stage at which when the investigation reaches it, it ends. It ends if it reaches the necessary (which is certainty that must be conceded by rational necessity), or if it reaches the probable which the opponent concedes. B - Result of the debate: If the proponent is unable to refute the objector's objection, the proponent is 'confounded' (mufham). If the objector is unable to correct his objection, he is 'cornered' (mulzam).
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage describes the structural stages of a valid debate and its possible outcomes. Every debate consists of three stages: first, the 'principles' stage where the point of contention is precisely defined to prevent talking past each other. Second, the 'intermediaries' stage where evidence is presented demonstrating the logical necessity of the claim. Third, the 'conclusions' stage where the debate terminates, either by reaching self-evident certainty (daruri) or by reaching a probable conclusion that the opponent accepts. The outcome is determined by the roles: if the proponent (mu'allil) fails to rebut an objection, he is 'confounded' (mufham); if the objector (sa'il) fails to sustain his objection, he is 'cornered' (mulzam).

Key Terms
المبادىء
الأواسط
المقاطع
الضروري
الظني
مفحماً
ملزماً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Stage of intermediaries
B) Stage of conclusions
C) Stage of principles
D) Stage of outcome

Key Takeaway
A valid debate has a clear structure: define the issue, present proofs, and reach a conclusion that either yields necessary certainty or a probable agreement; the outcome determines whether the proponent is confounded (mufham) or the objector is cornered (mulzam).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Meaningful Discourse: Simple and Sentence
Author classifies discourse into simple and compound.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given expression as mufrad or jumla, and further classify jumla as insha'iyya or khabariyya.

Core Idea
Discourse is either a concept or a complete sentence; only declarative sentences contain propositions.

This lesson introduces the basic classification of meaningful speech. Understanding whether an expression is a simple concept or a full proposition is essential for determining if it can be the subject of debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'mufrad'
Recognize that mufrad includes words (noun, verb, particle) and incomplete compounds like 'كتاب الله' and 'الرسول الصادق'.
Incomplete compounds are not full sentences.
2
Identify the term 'jumla'
A jumla is a complete sentence that conveys a full meaning and can stand alone.
The author divides jumla into two types.
3
Distinguish insha'iyya from khabariyya
Insha'iyya are performative (commands, wishes, questions), while khabariyya are declarative and contain a tasdiq (assent/proposition).
Only khabariyya have truth value.
4
Apply to examples
Take several Arabic expressions and classify them as mufrad, insha'iyya, or khabariyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا تَجْرِي فِيهِ المُنَاظَرَةُ وَمَا لَا تَجْرِي فِيهِ
ينقسم الكلام الدالُّ على معنىً إلى مفردٍ وجملة.
فالمفرد: هو كل ما كان من قبيل التصورات، فيدخل فيه الاسم والفعل والحرف، ويدخل فيه المركب الإضافي مثل: «كتابُ الله»، والمركب التقييدي مثل: «الرسول الصادق»، وكلُّ ما ليس بجملة تامة من المركبات الناقصة.
والجملة: كل كلام أفاد معنىً تاماً يصح السكوت عليه، وهي:
١ - إما أن تكون جملة إنشائية.
٢ - وإما أن تكون جملة خبرية، وهي الجملة التي تتضمن (تصديقاً) أي: القضية المنطقية التي حُكم فيها بإثبات أو نفي نسبة بين موضوع ومحمول (مسندٍ إليه ومسند).
والأصل في غير التصديق أن لا تجري فيه المناظرة، لأنه لا يشتمل على دعوى تكون محلاً للنزاع، أو يسوغ من الناحية الشكلية توجيه الاعتراض عليها، ولكن قد يستفسر عن معناه، أو عن المراد منه، وعند بيان المعنى أو بيان المراد يمكن توجيه الاعتراض، باعتبار أن ذلك تصديق قابل للاعتراض عليه.
فحين يستفسر (السائل) عن معنى كلمة، فيوضح (المعلّل) معناها، فإنه يكون قد قدّم بهذا التعريف اللفظي ادّعاء بأن معناها هو ما ذكره، عندئذٍ
What debate applies to and what it does not apply to:
Discourse signifying meaning is divided into simple and sentence.
The simple: is everything that is of the kind of concepts, and it includes the noun, verb, and particle, and includes the additive compound like 'Book of Allah', and the restrictive compound like 'the truthful messenger', and every incomplete compound that is not a complete sentence.
The sentence: every speech that conveys a complete meaning on which it is proper to be silent, and it is:
1 - Either it is a performative (insha'iyya) sentence.
2 - Or it is a declarative (khabariyya) sentence, which is the sentence that contains an assent (tasdiq), i.e., the logical proposition in which there is a judgment of affirmation or negation of a relation between a subject and a predicate (musnad ilayhi and musnad).
And the default regarding non-assent is that debate does not apply to it, because it does not contain a claim that is a place for dispute, or that formally allows directing an objection against it, but one may inquire about its meaning or what is intended by it, and upon clarification of the meaning or clarification of the intended, it becomes possible to direct an objection, considering that the clarification is an assent that is subject to objection.
So when the sa'il inquires about the meaning of a word, and the mu'allil clarifies its meaning, then he has presented by this verbal definition a claim that its meaning is what he mentioned; at that point...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage classifies types of discourse to determine what is and is not subject to debate. The author divides meaningful speech into simple (mufrad) and sentence (jumla). The simple includes words (noun, verb, particle) and incomplete compounds like 'Book of Allah' or 'the truthful messenger' — these are concepts (tasawwurat) without complete meaning. The sentence is a complete statement upon which one can stop. Sentences are either performative (insha'iyya: commands, wishes) or declarative (khabariyya), which contains a tasdiq (assent) — a logical proposition with a judgment of truth or falsehood. The principle is that debate properly applies to tasdiq, because only propositions contain a claim (da'wa) that can be disputed or objected to. Non-propositional utterances (like simple terms or performatives) are not directly debatable, but if someone asks about their meaning and the other clarifies, that clarification itself becomes a claim (e.g., 'the word means such-and-such') which is a proposition and thus open to objection. For instance, if a sa'il asks 'What is the meaning of salat?' and the mu'allil answers 'It means specific actions with takbir and salam,' this definition becomes a claim that can be challenged.

Key Terms
مفرد
جملة
جملة خبرية
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'بسم الله'
B) 'الحمد لله'
C) 'الصلاة خير من النوم'
D) 'الله أكبر'

Key Takeaway
Meaningful discourse is either a simple concept or a complete sentence; only declarative sentences are propositions suitable for debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Why Debate Is About Propositions, Not Concepts
Author explains what debate applies to and what it does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why debate does not directly apply to simple terms or performative sentences, and how clarifying meaning can turn a simple term into a debatable proposition.

Core Idea
Debate requires a claim; only tasdiq (propositions) have claims; concepts are not claims until defined.

This lesson builds on the classification to determine what can be debated. In Islamic dialectic, the primary subject of debate is the tasdiq. But even a simple term can become debatable when its meaning is clarified and becomes a propositional claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the default rule
The author states that the default (الأصل) is that debate does not apply to non-tasdiq.
Non-tasdiq includes mufrad and insha'iyya.
2
Reason for the rule
Non-tasdiq lacks a claim (دعوى) that can be a locus of dispute, and it does not formally allow directing an objection against it.
An objection requires a proposition to target.
3
Exception: clarification of meaning
When someone asks about the meaning of a simple term, the response becomes a claim about that meaning, which is a tasdiq and thus debatable.
The clarification is a verbal definition that asserts a specific meaning.
4
Apply to a scenario
Consider a sa'il asking: 'What is salat?' and the mu'allil replies: 'It is specific actions with takbir and salam.' This reply is now a proposition subject to objection.
The objection could be: 'The definition is incomplete because it omits intention (niyya).'

Uncovering Implied Claims in Non-Informative Speech
Author explains how non-tasdiq speech can imply a tasdiq, making it debatable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify implied assertive claims within non-assertive speech forms (commands, genitive constructs, restrictive compounds) and determine whether those claims are debatable.

Core Idea
Non-assertive speech becomes debatable when it implies a claim that can be affirmed or denied.

Not all speech that triggers debate is a direct statement of fact. Commands, contracts, and even grammatical constructions can hide claims that are open to challenge. This lesson shows you how to uncover those hidden claims and subject them to dialectical scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Identify the non-assertive speech form
Is it a performative sentence (command, contract), a genitive construct (e.g., 'lion's horns'), or a restrictive compound (e.g., 'the truthful messenger')? Recognize the grammatical category.
Performative sentences include commands, wishes, and legal contracts.
2
Extract the implied claim
Determine what assumption the form makes. A command implies the commander's authority. A genitive construct like 'lion's horns' implies that lions have horns. A restrictive compound like 'the truthful messenger' implies that the messenger is truthful and that the qualification is relevant.
The implied claim must be khabari (truth-apt) to be debatable.
3
Assess debatability
Can the implied claim be affirmed or denied? If yes, it can be debated. If no, it remains non-debatable.
Even a grammatical construction can be debated if it claims linguistic permissibility.
4
Formulate the objection
As a mu'tarid (objector), challenge the implied claim. For example, object to 'lion's horns' by asserting that lions do not have horns.
The objection must directly target the implied claim, not the form itself.
5
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how the implied claim affects the overall argument. For instance, in a proposition 'lion's horns are long,' the existence of horns is assumed; challenging it undermines the proposition.
This insight allows you to expand dialectical analysis to seemingly non-assertive expressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يتضمن غير التصديق معنى تصديقياً يفهم ضمناً، أو يفهم عن طريق اللزوم، فيسوغ توجيه الاعتراض عليه، وإجراء المناظرة فيه.
فالجمل الإنشائية: لا تجري المناظرة فيها، إلا باعتبار ما تتضمنه من حكم خبري، كاستحقاق الأمر أن يوجه أمره، وكاستحقاق منشىء العقد التصرف فيما أنشأه من عقد بيع، أو شراء، أو زواج، أو طلاق، أو عتق، أو نحو ذلك.
وحين تكون الجمل الإنشائية محكيَّة عن قائلها، فحكايتها ادعاء خبري، (للسائل) أن يطالب بتصحيح النقل فيه، ويجري المناظرة بناء على ذلك.
والمركب الإضافي: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء جواز مثل هذا التركيب لغة أو فكراً، أو باعتبار ما يشتمل عليه من دعاوى ضمنية، إذا كان جزءاً من قضية.
فقول القائل: «قرون الأسد» مركب إضافي يتضمن ادعاء بأن للأسد قروناً، فللسائل أن يعترض على هذا الادعاء ويُجري المناظرة فيه، إذا كان في قضية مثل: قرون الأسد طويلة.
وقول القائل: «حيثُ زيدٍ» بجر لفظة زيد، مركَّب إضافي يتضمن ادعاء بجواز مثل هذه الإضافة لغةً، فللمعترض أن يناظر في هذا الادعاء، وهذا يرجع إلى المناظرة في العبارة.
والمركب التقييدي: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء كونه قيداً للموضوع (المحكوم عليه)، أو قيداً للمحمول (المحكوم به)، أو قيداً للنسبة الحكمية، أو باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء جواز مثل هذا التركيب لغة أو فكراً.
And non-assent may include an assent-meaning understood implicitly, or understood by way of implication, so it is permissible to direct an objection against it and conduct debate in it. For performative sentences: debate is not conducted in them except by virtue of what they contain of a khabari ruling, like the deservingness of the commander to direct his command, and like the deservingness of the originator of a contract to dispose in what he originated of a contract of sale, purchase, marriage, divorce, emancipation, or the like. And when performative sentences are quoted from their speaker, their quotation is a khabari claim; the questioner may demand correction of the transmission therein, and debate proceeds based on that. The genitive construct: debate may be conducted in it by virtue of what it contains of a claim of permissibility of such a construction linguistically or intellectually, or by virtue of what it includes of implicit claims, if it is part of a proposition. So the saying of the speaker: 'lion's horns' is a genitive construct that contains a claim that the lion has horns; so the questioner may object to this claim and conduct debate in it, if it is in a proposition like: lion's horns are long. And the saying of the speaker: 'where Zayd' with the word Zayd in the genitive case is a genitive construct that contains a claim of permissibility of such an idafa linguistically; so the objector may debate this claim, and this goes back to debate in the expression. And the restrictive compound: debate may be conducted in it by virtue of what it contains of a claim that it is a restriction of the subject (the thing judged upon), or a restriction of the predicate (the thing judged with), or a restriction of the relational judgment, or by virtue of what it contains of a claim of permissibility of such a construction linguistically or intellectually.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage expands on the principle established earlier: although non-assent (non-tasdiq) is not directly debatable, it may contain or imply an assent-meaning, thereby becoming subject to dialectical objection. The author provides several categories and examples. First, performative sentences (إنشائيات) like commands or legal contracts: they are not directly debatable, but they imply a khabari (informative) claim, such as that the commander has authority or that the person making a contract has the right to do so. Additionally, when a performative sentence is quoted from its speaker, the act of quoting itself is a khabari claim ('he said such and such'), which can be debated for accuracy. Second, the genitive construct (مركب إضافي) may imply a claim: for example, 'lion's horns' implies that lions have horns; if used in a proposition like 'lion's horns are long,' the objector can challenge the existence of lion's horns. Another example is the genitive construction 'حيثُ زيدٍ' (where Zayd) – using the genitive case for 'Zayd' implies that such an idafa construction is permissible in Arabic; an objector can debate its linguistic validity. Third, the restrictive compound (مركب تقييدي) can be debated if it implies that the qualification (qayd) is correctly applied to the subject, predicate, or the relational judgment, or that such a restrictive construction is permissible. This passage shows that dialectical scrutiny can extend to expressions that are not complete propositions, as long as they entail a claim that can be affirmed or denied.

Key Terms
جمل إنشائية
مركب إضافي
مركب تقييدي
ادعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) By implying a khabari claim such as the commander's authority
C) By being quoted from someone
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Non-tasdiq speech can be debated if it entails a tasdiq, opening the door to dialectical engagement beyond explicit propositions.

Task 1: Find a performative sentence in a fiqh text (e.g., a command like 'establish prayer') and identify the implicit claim about the commander's authority. Then write an objection to that claim from a mu'tarid's perspective.

Implicit Declarative Judgment in Constructive Sentences
Author explains when constructive sentences can be debated via implied declarative judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit declarative judgment in a constructive sentence and determine whether it qualifies for dialectical debate.

Core Idea
Constructive sentences are debated not for their performance but for the declarative claims they imply.

In Islamic dialectic (munazara), not every sentence is directly debatable. Constructive sentences (إنشائيات) like commands and contracts perform actions rather than assert truths. However, as this passage explains, debate can proceed when such sentences contain an implicit declarative judgment. This lesson teaches you to extract that judgment and understand why it opens the door to debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the constructive sentence
Find a sentence that performs an action: command, request, contract (sale, marriage, etc.). These are not truth-apt themselves.
In Arabic grammar, inshā’ sentences are those that cannot be described as true or false.
2
Extract the implied declarative judgment
Ask: what must be true for this constructive sentence to be valid? For example, a command implies the commander has authority to command.
The author mentions استحقاق الأمر أن يوجه أمره (the entitlement of the commander to direct his command).
3
Determine if the judgment is debatable
Check if the implied judgment is itself a claim that can be true or false. If so, debate is permitted on that basis.
Debate does not occur on the constructive act itself but on the underlying entitlement or fact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالجمل الإنشائية: لا تجري المناظرة فيها، إلا باعتبار ما تتضمنه من حكم خبري، كاستحقاق الأمر أن يوجه أمره، وكاستحقاق منشىء العقد التصرف فيما أنشأه من عقد بيع، أو شراء، أو زواج، أو طلاق، أو عتق، أو نحو ذلك. وحين تكون الجمل الإنشائية محكيَّة عن قائلها، فحكايتها ادعاء خبري، (للسائل) أن يطالب بتصحيح النقل فيه، ويجري المناظرة بناء على ذلك.
Constructive sentences: debate does not occur in them except in consideration of what they contain of a declarative judgment, such as the entitlement of the commander to direct his command, and the entitlement of the contract maker to act in what he established of a sale contract, or purchase, or marriage, or divorce, or emancipation, or the like. And when constructive sentences are narrated from their speaker, their narration is a declarative claim; the questioner may demand correction of the transmission therein, and debate proceeds based on that.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage clarifies the status of constructive sentences (الجمل الإنشائية) in dialectical debate. The author states that such sentences are not directly debated as constructive utterances; rather, debate is allowed only to the extent they imply a declarative judgment. Examples include commands implying the commander's right to command, and contracts implying the contract maker's right to act. When constructive sentences are attributed to a speaker, the attribution itself becomes a declarative claim, thus the opponent can demand proof of correct transmission.

Key Terms
الجمل الإنشائية
حكم خبري
استحقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because they are written rather than spoken.

Key Takeaway
Constructive sentences are debatable only through the implicit declarative judgments they entail, not as performative acts themselves.

Debating Reported Constructive Sentences
Author explains that when constructive sentences are reported, the report becomes a declarative claim subject to debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a constructive sentence as a performative act and the same sentence when reported as a claim, and apply the rules of debate to the report.

Core Idea
When a constructive sentence is reported, it becomes a declarative claim that can be directly debated.

The previous lesson showed that constructive sentences imply declarative judgments. But what if someone narrates another person's constructive utterance? As the passage explains, the narration itself becomes a declarative claim (ادعاء خبري), and the opponent can demand verification of the transmission. This lesson focuses on that transformation.

Guided Path
1
Identify a reported constructive sentence
Look for a statement where speaker A attributes a constructive utterance to speaker B, e.g., 'He said to his wife: You are divorced.'
The report itself is a declarative sentence.
2
Recognize it as a declarative claim
The report claims that B actually uttered that sentence. That claim can be true or false.
The author says: فحكايتها ادعاء خبري (its narration is a declarative claim).
3
Apply the debate rule
The opponent (السائل) may demand proof of correct transmission (تصحيح النقل). If the report is unsupported, the debate can be halted or the claim rejected.
This is a standard procedure in munazara: challenging the authenticity of a reported statement.

Source Text

Constructive sentences: debate does not occur in them except in consideration of what they contain of a declarative judgment, such as the entitlement of the commander to direct his command, and the entitlement of the contract maker to act in what he established of a sale contract, or purchase, or marriage, or divorce, or emancipation, or the like. And when constructive sentences are narrated from their speaker, their narration is a declarative claim; the questioner may demand correction of the transmission therein, and debate proceeds based on that.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage clarifies the status of constructive sentences (الجمل الإنشائية) in dialectical debate. The author states that such sentences are not directly debated as constructive utterances; rather, debate is allowed only to the extent they imply a declarative judgment. Examples include commands implying the commander's right to command, and contracts implying the contract maker's right to act. Debating Implicit Claims in Incomplete Compounds
Author explains how compound constructions imply claims that can be debated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit declarative claims in genitive constructs and restrictive compounds and explain how they can be debated.

Core Idea
Incomplete compounds hide claims that can be challenged.

In dialectic, debate is not limited to full propositions; even incomplete constructions like 'the lion's horns' carry implicit claims that can be contested. This passage shows how genitive constructs (المركب الإضافي) and restrictive compounds (المركب التقييدي) imply statements about the world or about language itself. Understanding this opens new arenas for objection and defense.

Guided Path
1
Recognize genitive constructs
Identify a noun-noun idafa, such as 'قرون الأسد' (the lion's horns). This construction implies a claim that the second noun possesses the first, or that such a relation exists.
Also consider cases where the idafa might be linguistically questionable.
2
Extract the implicit claim
Ask what factual or linguistic assertion the compound implies. For 'قرون الأسد', the implicit claim is 'للأسد قرون' (the lion has horns). For 'حيثُ زيدٍ' (where Zaid's), the claim is that this idafa is permissible in Arabic.
The claim can be about reality or about language.
3
Distinguish restrictive compounds
Identify a construction that restricts the subject, predicate, or judgmental relation. For example, a limiting adjective or adverb. Determine what claim is being made by the restriction: e.g., that the restriction is appropriate for that subject or predicate.
The restriction can be on the subject (المحكوم عليه), predicate (المحكوم به), or the relation itself.
4
Generalize to all incomplete compounds
Apply the same logic to any incomplete compound. All such compounds carry declarative claims that can be debated. Recognize that the debate can focus on the linguistic permissibility or the factual truth of those claims.
This principle broadens the scope of dialectical objections beyond full sentences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمركب الإضافي: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء جواز مثل هذا التركيب لغة أو فكراً، أو باعتبار ما يشتمل عليه من دعاوى ضمنية، إذا كان جزءاً من قضية. فقول القائل: «قرون الأسد» مركب إضافي يتضمن ادعاء بأن للأسد قروناً، فللسائل أن يعترض على هذا الادعاء ويُجري المناظرة فيه، إذا كان في قضية مثل: قرون الأسد طويلة. وقول القائل: «حيثُ زيدٍ» بجر لفظة زيد، مركَّب إضافي يتضمن ادعاء بجواز مثل هذه الإضافة لغةً، فللمعترض أن يناظر في هذا الادعاء، وهذا يرجع إلى المناظرة في العبارة. والمركب التقييدي: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء كونه قيداً للموضوع (المحكوم عليه)، أو قيداً للمحمول (المحكوم به)، أو قيداً للنسبة الحكمية، أو باعتبار ما يتضمنه من ادعاء جواز مثل هذا التركيب لغة أو فكراً. وكذلك كل مركب ناقص: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من دعاوى خبرية.
And the genitive construct: debate may occur in it in consideration of what it contains of a claim of permissibility of such a construction in language or thought, or in consideration of what it includes of implicit claims, if it is part of a proposition. Thus the speaker's saying: 'The lion's horns' is a genitive construct that implies a claim that the lion has horns, so the questioner may object to this claim and conduct debate on it, if it is in a proposition such as: 'The lion's horns are long.' And the speaker's saying: 'where Zaid's' with the word Zaid in the genitive, is a genitive construct that implies a claim of permissibility of such an idafa in language, so the opponent may debate this claim, and this reverts to debate on expression. And the restrictive compound: debate may occur in it in consideration of what it contains of a claim of its being a restriction of the subject (the judged upon), or a restriction of the predicate (the judged with), or a restriction of the judgmental relation, or in consideration of what it contains of a claim of permissibility of such a construction in language or thought. And likewise every incomplete compound: debate may occur in it in consideration of what it contains of declarative claims.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage examines two types of incomplete compounds: the genitive construct (المركب الإضافي) and the restrictive compound (المركب التقييدي), and then generalizes to all incomplete compounds. The author explains that these constructions can imply claims either about their own linguistic permissibility or about factual matters. For example, 'The lion's horns' implies the claim that lions have horns; an opponent can challenge that. Similarly, 'where Zaid's' implies that the genitive construction is permissible in Arabic. The restrictive compound involves claims about what it restricts: subject, predicate, or relation. The key point is that even incomplete utterances can be debated for the declarative claims they imply.

Key Terms
المركب الإضافي
المركب التقييدي
مركب ناقص

Every incomplete compound carries implicit declarative claims that can be challenged in dialectic.
