Debating Definitions and Divisions in Islamic Dialectic
Author explains how definitions and divisions imply claims that can be debated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the claims embedded in definitions (musāwin, jāmiʿ, māniʿ) and divisions (actual inclusion, exhaustive coverage) and construct a dialectical objection against either.

Core Idea
Every definition or division is a hidden claim about reality—ready to be debated.

When a scholar defines a term or divides a category, they are not merely describing; they are making assertions that can be true or false. The author explains that definitions and divisions contain declarative claims that form the basis of dialectical debate. Understanding these claims is essential for critical engagement with Islamic scholarly discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim in a definition
A definition claims coextensiveness (musāwin) with the definiendum, comprehensiveness (jāmiʿ) covering all instances, and exclusiveness (māniʿ) excluding non-instances. At minimum, it claims suitability for identification.
2
Identify the claim in a division
A division claims that the listed parts actually fall under the divided category and that they exhaust all its parts (exhaustive enumeration).
3
Formulate a dialectical objection
For a definition, question its comprehensiveness: 'Does it include all instances?' or exclusiveness: 'Does it wrongly include non-instances?' For a division, question its exhaustiveness: 'Are there missing categories?'
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the division of 'word' into noun, verb, and particle. Challenge: 'Are there other parts of speech, like interjections?' Or define 'salāt' as specific actions and conditions: check if it includes all required components and excludes non-required acts.
The example from the text is used to illustrate how an opponent can debate the claim if it is not accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتعريفات: تجري المناظرة فيها باعتبارها تتضمن دعاوى خبرية تفيد أن هذا التعريف مساوٍ للمعرَّف، جامع مانع، أو صالح للتعريف به في أدنى المستويات. والتقسيمات: تجري المناظرة فيها باعتبارها تتضمن دعاوى خبرية تفيد أن هذه الأقسام مندرجة فعلاً تحت المَقْسِم، وأنها حاصرة لكل أقسامه، فالذي يقول مثلاً: «تنقسم الكلمة إلى اسم وفعل وحرف»، يُقدّم دعوى تتضمن أن هذه الأقسام هي أقسام الكلمة بطريقة حاصرة، فللمعترض أن يناظر في هذه الدعوى إذا لم يكن مُسَلَّماً بها.
And definitions: debate occurs in them in consideration of their containing declarative claims indicating that this definition is coextensive with the definiendum, comprehensive and exclusive, or suitable for defining it at the lowest levels. And divisions: debate occurs in them in consideration of their containing declarative claims indicating that these divisions are actually included under the divided, and that they encompass all its parts. So whoever says, for example: 'The word is divided into noun, verb, and particle,' presents a claim that these divisions are the divisions of the word in an encompassing manner; so the opponent may debate this claim if it is not accepted.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage addresses definitions and divisions as subjects of dialectical debate. The author states that definitions imply claims of being coextensive (musāwin) with the definiendum, comprehensive (jāmiʿ) and exclusive (māniʿ), or at least suitable for minimal identification. Divisions imply claims that the listed parts actually fall under the whole and that they exhaust all possibilities. The example of dividing 'word' into noun, verb, and particle illustrates a claim of exhaustive division; an opponent may challenge that if not conceded. The passage highlights that these activities, though seeming neutral, are laden with propositional content that can be attacked.

Key Terms
تعريف (taʿrīf): A definition that claims equivalence (musāwin) and comprehensiveness/exclusiveness (jāmiʿ māniʿ) [Note: Standard usage in logic; no significant shift.]
تقسيم (taqsīm): A division that claims actual inclusion and exhaustive coverage of the divided [Note: In logic, divisions are used for classification; here they are treated as claims.]
جامع مانع (jāmiʿ māniʿ): A definition that includes all instances and excludes non-instances [Note: Standard requirement for a proper definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a claim embedded in a definition?
A) That it is musāwin (coextensive)
B) That it is jāmiʿ (comprehensive)
C) That it is māniʿ (exclusive)
D) That it is agreed upon by all scholars

Key Takeaway
Definitions and divisions are not neutral—they are propositional claims that can be challenged in dialectic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'qiyās' (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh: 'It is the derivation of a ruling for an event without a textual basis from a similar event that has a textual basis.' Identify the claims of musāwin, jāmiʿ, māniʿ embedded in this definition and propose a dialectical objection against its comprehensiveness.
?
Take the division of 'knowledge' (ʿilm) into eternal and originated (qadīm vs ḥādith) as used by the mutakallimūn. What claims does this division make? Challenge whether it is exhaustive or if the parts are actually included under the whole.

Two Bases of Debate: Expressions and Transmitted Texts
Author explains expression and transmitted texts as bases for debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the roles of a transmitter and a claimant in a jadal debate and apply the principle 'in kuntu nāqilan fa-al-ṣiḥḥah, aw muddaʿiyan fa-al-dalīl' to real examples.

Core Idea
Your responsibility in debate depends on what you claim: transmission or truth.

In Islamic dialectic (jadal), every debate begins with a type of claim. The author distinguishes two categories: expressions (ʿibārah), which are scrutinized for linguistic correctness, and transmitted texts (nuṣūṣ manqūlah), where the focus is the authenticity of transmission. The famous maxim 'If you are a transmitter, then correctness; if a claimant, then proof' summarizes the entire procedure. Let us see how this principle works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Find in the Arabic passage the two categories of things that can be debated: expressions (عبارة) and transmitted texts (نصوص منقولة). Note the broad definition of expression: any utterance, whether word or sentence, definition, division, news, command, claim, or proof.
2
Determine the focus of debate for each category
For expressions, debate is about linguistic correctness (صحة من الناحية اللفظية). For transmitted texts, debate is about the correctness of transmission (صحة النقل). Notice that the content's truth is not initially at stake.
The text says: 'مناظرة راويها تنحصر بإثبات صحة النقل' – the debate of its narrator is confined to proving correctness of transmission.
3
Identify the roles: mustadill and māniʿ
The passage names the objector as 'mustadill' (مستدل) and the defender as 'māniʿ' (مانع). Associate these roles with the two categories: the mustadill challenges the linguistic correctness or the transmission; the māniʿ defends it.
These terms are standard in jadal literature.
4
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider a narrator who transmits a hadith. According to the principle, if he is merely transmitting, he is only responsible for correct attribution (transmission chain). If he also claims the content is true, then he becomes a 'claimant' and must provide proof. Use the quoted maxim to decide the burden of proof.
The maxim is: 'إن كنت ناقلاً فالصحة، أو مُدَّعِياً فالدليل'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبارة (وهي كل لفظ يصدر من المتكلّم، سواء أكان مفرداً أو جملة، وسواء أكان تعريفاً أو تقسيماً أو خبراً أو إنشاء أو دعوى أو دليلاً أو غير ذلك): تجري فيها المناظرة على أساس صحتها من الناحية اللفظية، فيرد عليها الاعتراض بالإبطال، بسبب كونها تخالف قواعد اللغة العربية مثلاً، ويكون الجواب بما يدفع الاعتراض من المبررات. واشتهر أن الذي يوجه الاعتراض على العبارة، أو التعريف أو التقسيم، يطلق عليه اسم (مُسْتَدِلّ). وأن المدافع صاحب العبارة أو التعريف، أو التقسيم، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع). والنصوص المنقولة: (مهما كان مضمونها خبرياً أو إنشائياً مفرداً أو جملة) تتضمن ادّعاءً بصحة النقل عن قائلها، ومناظرة راويها تنحصر بإثبات صحة النقل، أما إثبات صحة المضمون فليس مسؤولاً عنه، ما لم يلتزم الراوي ادّعاء صحته، فهو عندئذٍ مدَّعٍ صحة المضمون، فتجري المناظرة معه على أنه مدّعٍ للقضية أو القضايا الخبرية التي اشتمل عليها النص. وفي هذا يقول علماء هذا الفن: «إن كنت ناقلاً فالصحة، أو مُدَّعِياً فالدليل».
And the expression (and it is every utterance issued by the speaker, whether it is a single word or a sentence, and whether it is a definition or a division or a piece of news or a construction or a claim or a proof or other than that): debate occurs in it on the basis of its correctness from the verbal perspective, so objection is made against it by invalidation, due to it contradicting the rules of the Arabic language, for example, and the answer is whatever repels the objection of justifications. And it is well-known that the one who directs the objection to the expression, or definition or division, is called (mustadill), and the defender, the owner of the expression, definition, or division, is called (māniʿ). And transmitted texts: (whatever their content, declarative or constructive, single word or sentence) imply a claim of correctness of transmission from their speaker, and the debate of its narrator is confined to proving the correctness of the transmission; as for proving the correctness of the content, he is not responsible for it, unless the narrator commits to claiming its correctness, then at that time he is a claimant of the correctness of the content, so debate occurs with him as a claimant of the proposition or propositions contained in the text. And concerning this, the scholars of this art say: 'If you are a transmitter, then [demand] correctness; or if a claimant, then proof.'
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage covers two final categories: expressions and transmitted texts. Expressions are any utterances; debate focuses on their linguistic correctness, e.g., violation of Arabic grammar. The objector is called mustadill, the defender māniʿ. Transmitted texts imply a claim of correct attribution; the narrator's responsibility is only the accuracy of transmission, not the truth of content, unless he claims it. The famous principle is quoted: 'If you are a transmitter, then correctness [of transmission]; if a claimant, then [bring] proof.' This summarizes the entire discussion: the basis of debate depends on the type of claim.

Key Terms
عبارة
نصوص منقولة
مستدل
مانع
إن كنت ناقلاً فالصحة، أو مُدَّعِياً فالدليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) إثبات صحة النقل فقط
C) إثبات صحة اللغة
D) إثبات الدليل على كل قضية

Key Takeaway
In jadal, the burden of proof shifts: a transmitter only needs to prove correct transmission; a claimant of content must bring evidence.

Types of Statements Debated in Munazara
Author explains that definitions, divisions, expressions, and transmitted texts involve assertive claims and can be debated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of statements subject to debate in munazara and distinguish between the roles of mustadill and mani'.

Core Idea
Definitions, divisions, expressions, and transmitted texts all contain implicit claims that can be contested.

In a dialectical debate (munazara), not every utterance is automatically debatable—only those that assert a claim. This passage from the Dawabit al-Ma'rifa clarifies exactly which types of statements are subject to debate and why. By understanding this classification, you will be able to analyze any dialectical exchange and determine what is being contested and by whom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Find and list the four types of statements the passage mentions: definitions (ta'rifat), divisions (taqsimat), expressions ('ibarat), and transmitted texts (nusus manqula).
These are not the only possible statements, but they cover the main categories in munazara.
2
Extract the implicit claim in each
For each category, determine what assertive claim (da'wa khabariyya) is being made. A definition claims equivalence to the definiendum and comprehensiveness; a division claims that the parts are actual and exhaustive; an expression claims linguistic correctness; a transmitted text claims authenticity of transmission.
Understanding the implicit claim is key to formulating an objection.
3
Determine the ground for objection
Note the specific basis on which each type can be objected to: definitions and divisions can be challenged for inaccuracy or incompleteness; expressions for violating language rules; transmitted texts for lack of authentic transmission.
The objector (mustadill) must target the specific claim.
4
Identify the roles
The passage assigns roles: the objector is called mustadill, the defender is mani'. For transmitted texts, the narrator is only responsible for transmission unless he also claims the content's truth.
The burden of proof shifts depending on the role.
5
Apply the maxim
The passage concludes with the rule: 'If you are a transmitter, then correctness; if a claimant, then evidence.' Explain how this rule governs the burden of proof in debates about transmitted texts.
This maxim is frequently cited in later dialectical works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك كل مركب ناقص: قد تجري المناظرة فيه باعتبار ما يتضمنه من دعاوى خبرية. والتعريفات: تجري المناظرة فيها باعتبارها تتضمن دعاوى خبرية تفيد أن هذا التعريف مساوٍ للمعرَّف، جامع مانع، أو صالح للتعريف به في أدنى المستويات. والتقسيمات: تجري المناظرة فيها باعتبارها تتضمن دعاوى خبرية تفيد أن هذه الأقسام مندرجة فعلاً تحت المَقْسِم، وأنها حاصرة لكل أقسامه، فالذي يقول مثلاً: «تنقسم الكلمة إلى اسم وفعل وحرف»، يُقدّم دعوى تتضمن أن هذه الأقسام هي أقسام الكلمة بطريقة حاصرة، فللمعترض أن يناظر في هذه الدعوى إذا لم يكن مُسَلَّماً بها. والعبارة (وهي كل لفظ يصدر من المتكلّم، سواء أكان مفرداً أو جملة، وسواء أكان تعريفاً أو تقسيماً أو خبراً أو إنشاء أو دعوى أو دليلاً أو غير ذلك): تجري فيها المناظرة على أساس صحتها من الناحية اللفظية، فيرد عليها الاعتراض بالإبطال، بسبب كونها تخالف قواعد اللغة العربية مثلاً، ويكون الجواب بما يدفع الاعتراض من المبررات. واشتهر أن الذي يوجه الاعتراض على العبارة، أو التعريف أو التقسيم، يطلق عليه اسم (مُسْتَدِلّ). وأن المدافع صاحب العبارة أو التعريف، أو التقسيم، يطلق عليه اسم (مانع). والنصوص المنقولة: (مهما كان مضمونها خبرياً أو إنشائياً مفرداً أو جملة) تتضمن ادّعاءً بصحة النقل عن قائلها، ومناظرة راويها تنحصر بإثبات صحة النقل، أما إثبات صحة المضمون فليس مسؤولاً عنه، ما لم يلتزم الراوي ادّعاء صحته، فهو عندئذٍ مدَّعٍ صحة المضمون، فتجري المناظرة معه على أنه مدّعٍ للقضية أو القضايا الخبرية التي اشتمل عليها النص. وفي هذا يقول علماء هذا الفن: «إن كنت ناقلاً فالصحة، أو مُدَّعِياً فالدليل».
Likewise, every incomplete compound: debate may occur in it considering what it contains of assertive claims. Definitions: debate occurs in them considering that they contain assertive claims indicating that this definition is equivalent to the definiendum, comprehensive and exclusive, or suitable for defining at the lowest levels. Divisions: debate occurs in them considering that they contain assertive claims indicating that these divisions are actually subsumed under the divided genus, and that they encompass all its parts. For example, one who says: 'The word is divided into noun, verb, and particle' puts forward a claim that these parts are the parts of the word in an encompassing manner. Thus the objector may debate this claim if it is not accepted. The expression (which is every utterance emanating from the speaker, whether single word or sentence, and whether a definition, division, report, command, claim, proof, or otherwise): debate occurs in it based on its correctness from the lexical perspective. So objection is raised against it by rebuttal, due to it contravening the rules of the Arabic language, for example. And the answer is by responding with justifications that repel the objection. It is well-known that the one who directs the objection to the expression, definition, or division is called 'mustadill' (objector). And the defender, the author of the expression, definition, or division, is called 'mani'' (defender). Transmitted texts (whatever their content, report or command, single word or sentence) include a claim of the correctness of the transmission from its speaker. The debate of its narrator is confined to proving the correctness of the transmission. As for proving the correctness of the content, he is not responsible for it, unless the narrator commits to claiming its correctness. Then he is at that time a claimant of the correctness of the content, so debate occurs with him as a claimant of the proposition(s) that the text contained. Regarding this, the scholars of this art say: 'If you are a transmitter, then correctness; or if a claimant, then evidence.'
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of statements that can be subjected to munazara (dialectical debate). The author begins by noting that even incomplete compound statements can be debated because they contain assertive claims. Then he discusses four categories. First, definitions: a definition asserts that it is equivalent to the definiendum, comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'), or at least suitable for identification. Second, divisions: a division asserts that the listed parts are actual subdivisions of the whole and that they exhaust it (hasira). The example given is the division of kalima into ism, fi'l, and harf. Third, expression (al-'ibara): any uttered wording is examined for linguistic correctness. The objection (naqd) takes the form of rebuttal (ibṭal) based on violation of language rules, and the answer is a justification. Fourth, transmitted texts (nusus manqula): the debate focuses on the authenticity of transmission, not the content's truth, unless the narrator also claims the content's correctness. The passage concludes with a maxim: 'If you are a transmitter, then correctness; if a claimant, then evidence.' This emphasizes that the burden of proof differs depending on whether one is merely reporting or asserting a position. The passage also introduces key roles: objector (mustadill) and defender (mani'). This is foundational for understanding the structure of a dialectical exchange.

Key Terms
مناظرة
دعوى خبرية
مستدل
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Proving the truth of the content
B) Proving the correctness of the transmission
C) Examining its linguistic structure
D) Assessing its compatibility with definitions

Key Takeaway
In munazara, the types of statements subject to debate are definitions, divisions, expressions, and transmitted texts, each with its own basis for objection and burden of proof.

Debate about Expression: Definition and Procedure
Author defines expression in debate and outlines the objection/response procedure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the definition of ‘ibāra in debate and identify the steps in objecting to and defending an expression on linguistic grounds.

Core Idea
Expression in debate is any utterance; objections target linguistic correctness, not truth.

In Islamic scholarly debate, arguments are evaluated not only for content but also for wording. This lesson introduces the debate about expression (al-‘ibāra), where the focus is on whether a statement conforms to Arabic linguistic norms. You will learn how an objector can challenge a defender's phrasing and how the defender can respond according to established rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of ‘ibāra
The passage defines ‘ibāra as any utterance from the speaker, including definitions, divisions, claims, proofs, etc. Note the breadth: it covers all verbal statements in debate.
2
Recognize the grounds for objection
There are three grounds for rebuttal: violation of a grammatical rule, violation of established linguistic practice, or the word having no basis for the intended meaning.
These grounds are linguistic, not logical or substantive.
3
Trace the defender's response
The defender may either justify his wording by citing an accepted opinion (even if from only some scholars of Arabic) or concede the error and correct his expression.
Justification must be based on recognized authority, not personal preference.
4
Distinguish linguistic from substantive debate
Understand that debate about expression concerns the correctness of wording, not the truth of the claim. This distinction prevents confusion in scholarly discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني ضَوَابِطُ المُنَاظَرَةِ وَتَطْبِيقَاتُ (١) المُنَاظَرَةُ فِي العِبَارَةِ العبارة في اصطلاح علماء هذا الفن هي: مطلق اللفظ الصادر من المتكلم، سواء أكان تعريفاً، أو تقسيماً، أو دعوى، أو دليلاً، أو غير ذلك. وتجري المناظرة في العبارة على الوجه التالي: يُوجّه (المستدلُّ) على عبارة (المانع) الإبطال، بسبب أنها تخالف قاعدة من قواعد اللغة، أو تخالف الضبط اللغوي المتبع، أو أن الكلمة لا أصل لها على المعنى الذي قصده صاحب العبارة (المانع). فيجيب المانع ببيان الوجه الذي استند إليه في عبارته، إذا كان له وجه في ذلك، أو يسلّم الاعتراض ويُصحّح عبارته. وتوجيه المانع عبارته ينبغي أن يستند فيه إلى رأي مقبول ولو عند بعض أئمة العربية.
Chapter Two: Rules of Debate and Applications (1) Debate about Expression. Expression in the terminology of the scholars of this art is: any utterance emanating from the speaker, whether it is a definition, division, claim, proof, or otherwise. Debate about expression proceeds as follows: The objector directs rebuttal against the defender's expression, because it contravenes a rule of grammar, or contravenes the established linguistic standard, or because the word has no basis for the meaning intended by the author of the expression (the defender). Then the defender responds by explaining the basis he relied upon in his expression, if he has a basis for it, or he concedes the objection and corrects his expression. And the defender's justification of his expression should be based on an accepted opinion, even if only according to some of the imams of Arabic.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage opens the second chapter of the book on the rules of debate (ḍawābiṭ al-munāẓara) and its applications. The first subsection deals with debate about expression (al-‘ibāra). First, it defines 'expression' in this context: any utterance from the speaker, regardless of type — definition, division, claim, proof, etc. This definition is intentionally broad, covering all verbal statements. Next, it outlines the procedure: the objector (mustadill) raises a rebuttal (ibṭāl) against the defender's (māni‘) expression, citing a violation of a language rule, a deviation from the standard linguistic practice, or the word's lack of a known basis for the intended meaning. The defender then has two options: either justify his wording by showing an acceptable linguistic basis, or concede the error and correct it. The passage emphasizes that the defender's justification should be grounded in a recognized opinion, even if it is only accepted by some scholars of Arabic. This shows that the debate about expression is not arbitrary; it refers to established linguistic norms. The procedure ensures that linguistic precision is maintained in scholarly discourse. Importantly, the debate here is not about the truth of the content but about the correctness of the wording itself.

Key Terms
عبارة
إبطال
ضوابط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) A consensus of all grammarians

D) The literal dictionary meaning

Key Takeaway
Debate about expression focuses on linguistic correctness, not truth; the defender must justify his wording with accepted linguistic authority.

What is Debate on Expression? Definition and Procedure
Author defines debate on expression and illustrates with two linguistic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'ibara in the context of munazara and describe the roles of mustadill and mani' in a debate on expression.

Core Idea
Debate on expression scrutinizes linguistic correctness before any other argument.

In Islamic dialectical tradition, not all disputes are about substantive issues; many revolve around the very wording of a claim. This lesson introduces the concept of 'ibara and the procedure by which a challenger can object to a claimant's expression.

Guided Path
1
Define 'ibara
Locate the author's definition: 'العبارة في اصطلاح علماء هذا الفن هي: مطلق اللفظ الصادر من المتكلم'. Note that it includes definitions, divisions, claims, proofs, etc.
2
Identify the two parties
The mustadill is the challenger who objects; the mani' is the claimant who made the statement. Understand their roles.
In other texts, the terms may be reversed.
3
Explore grounds for objection
The challenger can object because: (1) the statement violates a grammar rule, (2) it violates accepted linguistic usage, (3) a word has no lexical basis for the intended meaning.
4
Understand the claimant's response
The claimant may either defend his wording by citing a recognized authority or concede and correct.
The defence must rely on an acceptable opinion, even if minority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني
ضَوَابِطُ المُنَاظَرَةِ وَتَطْبِيقَاتُ
(١)
المُنَاظَرَةُ فِي العِبَارَةِ
العبارة في اصطلاح علماء هذا الفن هي: مطلق اللفظ الصادر من المتكلم، سواء أكان تعريفاً، أو تقسيماً، أو دعوى، أو دليلاً، أو غير ذلك.
وتجري المناظرة في العبارة على الوجه التالي:
يُوجّه (المستدلُّ) على عبارة (المانع) الإبطال، بسبب أنها تخالف قاعدة من قواعد اللغة، أو تخالف الضبط اللغوي المتبع، أو أن الكلمة لا أصل لها على المعنى الذي قصده صاحب العبارة (المانع).
فيجيب المانع ببيان الوجه الذي استند إليه في عبارته، إذا كان له وجه في ذلك، أو يسلّم الاعتراض ويُصحّح عبارته.
وتوجيه المانع عبارته ينبغي أن يستند فيه إلى رأي مقبول ولو عند بعض أئمة العربية.
أمثلة:
أ ـ المانع: (صاحب العبارة) قال: يقطف صاحب الكرم من أعناقها عناقيده.
المستدل: هذه العبارة خطأ، لأن فيها عود الضمير على متأخر لفظاً ورتبة.
والقاعدة العربية تقضي بأنه لا يعود الضمير إلا على متقدّم في اللفظ أو في الرتبة، أو فيهما معاً، أما المتأخر في اللفظ والرتبة معاً فلا يجوز عَوْد الضمير عليه .
المانع : أسلم الاعتراض، وأصحح العبارة فأقول: يقطف صاحب الكرم عناقيده من أعناقها .
ب ـ المانع : (صاحب العبارة) قال : إنما يخشى ربهم العلماء .
المستدل : هذه العبارة خطأ، لأن فيها عود الضمير على متأخر .
المانع : الاعتراض ممنوع، لأن المتأخر في هذه العبارة هو متقدّم في الرتبة، إذ هو فاعل (يخشى) والفاعل رتبته متقدمة على رتبة المفعول به .
ومثل هذا جائز شائع .
Chapter Two: Rules of Debate and Applications (1) Debate Concerning the Utterance. The utterance in the terminology of the scholars of this art is: absolute speech emanating from the speaker, whether it is a definition, a division, a claim, a proof, or other than that. And the debate concerning the utterance proceeds as follows: The challenger directs invalidation against the claimant's statement, because it violates a rule of grammar, or violates the accepted linguistic standard, or that the word has no basis for the meaning intended by the owner of the statement (the claimant). Then the claimant responds by explaining the basis he relied on in his statement, if he has a basis for that, or he concedes the objection and corrects his statement. And the claimant's justification of his statement should rely on an acceptable opinion, even if it is only according to some of the leading scholars of Arabic. Examples: A. The claimant (the owner of the statement) said: 'The owner of the vineyard picks from its branches his clusters.' The challenger: This statement is wrong, because it contains the pronoun referring back to something delayed in word and rank. And the Arabic rule dictates that the pronoun only refers back to something preceding in word or in rank, or in both together. As for something delayed in both word and rank together, it is not permissible for the pronoun to refer back to it. The claimant: I concede the objection and correct the statement, so I say: 'The owner of the vineyard picks his clusters from its branches.' B. The claimant (the owner of the statement) said: 'Indeed, their Lord fears the scholars.' The challenger: This statement is wrong, because it contains the pronoun referring back to something delayed. The claimant: The objection is rejected, because that which is delayed in this statement is advanced in rank, for it is the subject of 'fears' and the subject's rank is advanced over the rank of the object. And such [usage] is permissible and common.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage defines a specific type of dialectical debate (munazara) that focuses on the expression ('ibara) used by a disputant. It opens by defining 'ibara as any utterance from a speaker, whether it be a definition, division, claim, evidence, etc. Then it describes the standard process: the challenger (mustadill) attacks the claimant's (mani') statement on linguistic grounds – either it violates a grammatical rule, deviates from accepted usage, or the word has no lexical basis for the intended meaning. The claimant may either defend his wording by citing a recognized linguistic opinion (even if minority) or concede and correct the error. Two illustrative examples from Arabic grammar are given. In the first, the claimant says: 'yqtifu sahibu al-karmi min a'naqiha 'anaqidahu' (The owner of the vineyard picks from its branches his clusters). The challenger points out that the pronoun -ha in 'a'naqiha' refers to the vineyard, but the noun 'a'naq' is delayed both in word order and in rank (it is a prepositional phrase, which is grammatically subordinate). The rule is that a pronoun cannot refer back to an antecedent that is delayed in both word and rank. The claimant concedes and rephrases: 'yqtifu sahibu al-karmi 'anaqidahu min a'naqiha' (The owner of the vineyard picks his clusters from its branches), placing the object 'anaqidahu before the prepositional phrase, thus making the antecedent clear and proper. In the second example, the claimant says: 'innama yakhsha rabbahum al-'ulama'' (Indeed, their Lord fears the scholars). The challenger again objects that the pronoun -hum in 'rabbahum' refers to 'al-'ulama'', which is delayed in word order. The claimant refutes this by explaining that although 'al-'ulama' is delayed in word, it is the subject (fa'il) of the verb 'yakhsha', and the subject is grammatically advanced in rank over the object. Therefore, it is permissible and common for a pronoun to refer to a word that is delayed in pronunciation but advanced in grammatical rank, as here the subject precedes the object in rank. This passage teaches that linguistic precision is crucial in dialectical exchange, and that one must be familiar with both surface word order and underlying grammatical hierarchy.

المستدل
المانع

D) Only linguistic expressions free of error.

Key Takeaway
Debate on expression focuses on the linguistic correctness of the utterance, and the claimant must either defend or correct it.

Analysing Two Linguistic Objections in Debate
Author defines debate on expression and illustrates with two linguistic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse a debate on expression by identifying the linguistic error, the rule violated, and the validity of the defence.

Core Idea
Grammatical rank can override word order in pronoun reference.

In the previous lesson, you learned the general procedure. Now we examine two concrete examples from Arabic grammar to see how the challenger's objection and the claimant's response are handled.

Guided Path
1
Read the first example
The claimant says: 'يقطف صاحب الكرم من أعناقها عناقيده'. Identify the pronoun -ha in 'أعناقها' and its antecedent.
2
Check the linguistic rule
The challenger says the pronoun refers to something delayed in both word and rank. Explain: 'أعناق' is a prepositional phrase, grammatically subordinate, so it is delayed in rank.
Rule: a pronoun cannot refer to something delayed in both word and rank.
3
Observe the correction
The claimant corrects to: 'يقطف صاحب الكرم عناقيده من أعناقها'. Now the pronoun -ha in 'أعناقها' has a clear antecedent that is closer.
4
Analyse the second example
The claimant says: 'إنما يخشى ربهم العلماء'. The challenger objects again on pronoun reference.
5
Evaluate the defence
The claimant argues that although 'العلماء' is delayed in word, it is the subject of the verb, which is advanced in rank over the object. Hence the pronoun 'هم' is permissible.
This illustrates that grammatical rank can override word order.

Pronoun Return and Dialectical Analysis in Arabic Grammar
Author explains the rule of pronoun return and presents two dialectical exchanges on its application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange on pronoun return by distinguishing wording and rank, and apply the rule to new Arabic sentences.

Core Idea
Pronouns can refer to later nouns if the noun is earlier in logical rank, even if later in wording.

This passage presents a grammatical rule about pronoun reference along with two dialectical challenges. Understanding the distinction between linguistic order (wording) and logical priority (rank) is crucial for interpreting Arabic texts, especially in tafsir and usul al-fiqh where pronoun antecedents must be correctly identified. The dialectical format shows how objections are raised and answered using this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
The author states that a pronoun cannot refer to a noun that is later in both wording and rank. Read the rule carefully: it sets the boundary for permissible pronoun return.
The rule is about 'al-muta'akhkhir fi al-lafz wa al-rutba ma'an'.
2
Examine the first objection and response
The first objector (mani') pretends to correct the expression by saying 'The owner of the vineyard picks its clusters from its vines.' Analyze which pronoun refers to what: 'its clusters' and 'its vines' both refer to the vineyard, which is later in wording but earlier in rank (owner).
The objector is actually showing a permissible case.
3
Examine the second objection and response
The second example: 'Only the scholars fear their Lord.' The pronoun 'their' refers to 'Lord' which appears later in wording. However, the objector argues that 'Lord' is the subject (fa'il) of 'fears', which has a higher rank than the object 'scholars'. Thus, it is advanced in rank.
Rank here is grammatical priority: subject > object.
4
Distinguish wording and rank
Summarize the principle: wording (lafz) is the order of words in the sentence; rank (rutba) is the logical or grammatical priority (e.g., subject before object, possessor before possessed). A pronoun can refer to a noun that is later in wording if that noun is earlier in rank.
This distinction is key to resolving the objections.
5
Apply the rule to a new example
Take an Arabic sentence like 'قَرَأَ الطَّالِبُ كِتَابَهُ' (The student read his book). The pronoun 'hu' refers to 'student', which is earlier in wording and rank, so it's fine. Now consider: 'كِتَابَهُ قَرَأَ الطَّالِبُ' – here the pronoun 'hu' refers to 'student' which is later in wording but earlier in rank (subject). Is this permissible? Yes, by the same logic.
This exercise reinforces the rule's application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الرتبة، أو فيهما معاً، أما المتأخر في اللفظ والرتبة معاً فلا يجوز عَوْد الضمير عليه .
المانع : أسلم الاعتراض، وأصحح العبارة فأقول: يقطف صاحب الكرم عناقيده من أعناقها .
ب ـ المانع : (صاحب العبارة) قال : إنما يخشى ربهم العلماء .
المستدل : هذه العبارة خطأ، لأن فيها عود الضمير على متأخر .
المانع : الاعتراض ممنوع، لأن المتأخر في هذه العبارة هو متقدّم في الرتبة، إذ هو فاعل (يخشى) والفاعل رتبته متقدمة على رتبة المفعول به .
ومثل هذا جائز شائع .
In rank, or in both together. As for what is later in wording and rank together, it is not permissible for the pronoun to return to it.
The objector: I concede the objection, and I correct the expression, so I say: The owner of the vineyard picks its clusters from its vines.
B – The objector: (The author of the expression) said: 'Only the scholars fear their Lord.'
The respondent: This expression is wrong, because in it the pronoun returns to something later.
The objector: The objection is not allowed, because what is later in this expression is advanced in rank, since it is the subject (agent) of 'fears', and the subject's rank is prior to the rank of the object. And such is permissible and common.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is dialectical, presenting a rule about pronoun reference and two objections with responses. The rule states: a pronoun cannot refer back to a noun that is later in both wording and rank (رتبة). The first example is an apparent correction: 'the owner of the vineyard picks its clusters from its vines' – the pronoun 'its' refers to 'vineyard' which is later in wording but earlier in rank (the vineyard owns the clusters). The second example is a Quranic-like phrase: 'Only the scholars fear their Lord.' Here, the pronoun 'their' refers to 'Lord' mentioned later in wording, but the Lord is the subject (agent) of 'fears', which is earlier in rank than the object 'scholars'. The respondent argues that such usage is permissible because the antecedent is advanced in rank despite being later in wording. The passage illustrates that in disputation, one must carefully distinguish between wording and logical rank.

Transmission in Disputation: Definition and Rules
Author defines transmission and explains transmitter's obligations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define transmission (naql) in disputation and determine when a questioner may object to a transmitted statement.

Core Idea
Transmission in disputation becomes a claim if the reporter commits to its truth.

This passage introduces a crucial concept in adab al-bahth: al-naql (transmission). Before you can engage in any disputation involving cited authorities, you must understand what counts as a valid transmission and what rights the opponent has to challenge it. The author defines naql broadly and then distinguishes two scenarios based on the transmitter's commitment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of naql
Find the sentence beginning with 'النقل: هو...' (line 2). Note the two components: the act (bringing the speech of another) and the condition (showing attribution to its speaker). The definition explicitly covers definitions, divisions, assents, etc.
This definition is broader than hadith transmission; it applies to any statement in a disputation.
2
Classify the transmitter's stance
Determine whether the transmitter adds a commitment to the truth of the content (e.g., 'هذا هو الحكم الصحيح') or merely quotes without endorsement. The examples show both: the poetry and the Shafi'i ruling may be quoted with or without commitment.
If the transmitter commits, he becomes a claimant (mudda'i) and is subject to disputation on correctness.
3
Identify when the questioner may object
If the transmitter does not commit, check the three exceptions: self-evident (badihi), accepted by opponent (musallam bih), or necessary to opponent's school (daruri min madhhabihi). If any applies, the questioner cannot object. Otherwise, the questioner may demand proof of the transmission's authenticity.
These exceptions protect well-known or conceded statements from unnecessary challenges.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Consider a Hanbali who quotes a hadith without endorsing it. The Hanafi opponent denies the hadith's authenticity. Determine whether the Hanbali must prove it or if the opponent cannot object because the hadith is self-evident (e.g., widely transmitted).
Use the three criteria from the text to decide.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(2)
المُنَاظَرَةُ فِي النَّقْلِ
النقل: هو إتيان الإنسان بكلام لغيره، مع إظهار إسناده إلى قائله، تعريفاً كان أو تقسيماً أو تصديقاً أو غير ذلك.
كقول الناقل: قال أبو الطيب المتنبي:
يا من يعزُّ علينا أن نفارقهم وجداننا كلَّ شيء بعدكم عدم
إن كان سرَّكُم ما قال حاسدنا فما لجرحٍ إذا أرضاكم ألمُ
وكقول الناقل: قال الشافعية:
لمس الرجل المرأة الأجنبية دون حائل ينقض الوضوء.
فإن التزم الناقل صحة مضمون الكلام الذي نقله، فقال مثلاً: وقول الشافعية هو الحكم الصحيح، فهو مدَّعٍ، ويُناظر على أساس أنه مدَّع ضمن ضوابط المناظرة في التصديق. أو قال مثلاً في قول أبي الطيب المتنبي:
هذي برزتِ لنا فهجتِ رسیـسا ثم انثَنَيْتِ وما شفيت نسيسا
وعبارة المتنبي هذه صحيحة لا اعتراض عليها، فهو بمثابة صاحب عبارة يناظرُ ضمن أصول المناظرة في العبارة.
أما إذا لم يلتزم صحة مضمون النقل: فإن كان النقل بديهياً، أو مسلماً به عند الخصم، أو معتبراً من ضروريات مذهبه، فليس (للسائل) أن يوجه إليه أي اعتراض. وإن كان النقل غير ذلك، كان للسائل أن يطالب بتصحيح النقل، أي: أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل وفق طرائق إثبات المرويات.
(2)
The Disputation regarding Transmission
Transmission: It is a person's bringing the speech of another, while showing its attribution to its speaker, whether it is a definition, a division, an assent, or otherwise.
Like the transmitter's saying: Abu al-Tayyib al-Mutanabbi said:
'O you whom it is hard for us to leave, we find everything after you is nothing.
If what our envious said has pleased you, then for a wound that pleases you there is no pain.'
And like the transmitter's saying: The Shafi'is said:
'Touching a foreign woman without a barrier nullifies ablution.'
If the transmitter commits to the correctness of the content of the speech he transmitted, and says for example: 'The Shafi'is' statement is the correct ruling,' then he is a claimant, and he is debated on the basis that he is a claimant within the rules of disputation regarding assent. Or if he says, for example, regarding Abu al-Tayyib al-Mutanabbi's statement:
'She appeared to us and stirred my grief, then she turned away and did not quench my burning thirst,
and this expression of al-Mutanabbi is correct and there is no objection to it,' then he is like an author of an expression who debates within the principles of disputation regarding expression.
As for if he does not commit to the correctness of the content of the transmission: if the transmission is self-evident, or accepted by the opponent, or deemed necessary to his school, then it is not for the questioner to direct any objection to it. And if the transmission is otherwise, then the questioner may demand the correction of the transmission, i.e., demand proof of its correctness according to the methods of proving transmitted reports.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage defines a new concept in disputation: al-naql (transmission). The author defines naql as citing another person's speech with attribution to its speaker, covering definitions, divisions, assents, etc. Two examples are given: poetry by al-Mutanabbi and a legal ruling by the Shafi'is. The key distinction is whether the transmitter commits to the truth of the content. If he does commit, he becomes a claimant (mudda'i) and faces disputation rules for tasdiq (assent). If he does not commit, the transmission is merely informative. However, even without commitment, if the transmitted content is self-evident (badihi), accepted by the opponent, or essential to the opponent's school, the questioner cannot object; otherwise, the questioner can demand verification of the transmission's authenticity using standard methods of hadith criticism. This passage is crucial for understanding how statements from authorities are handled in Islamic dialectic.

Key Terms
النقل
مدع
السائل
بديهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a transmitter quotes a Shafi'i ruling and says 'This is the correct ruling,' what is his status in the disputation?
A) He is a mere transmitter, no disputation applies.
B) He is a claimant (mudda'i) and must defend the ruling.
C) He is the author of the statement.
D) He is the questioner.

Key Takeaway
Transmission in disputation is defined as attributing speech to its speaker; the transmitter becomes a claimant if he endorses the content, and the questioner can only challenge a non-endorsed transmission if it is not self-evident, accepted, or necessary to the opponent's school.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Scenario: A Maliki says: 'Imam Malik said: "Selling food before taking possession is not allowed." Then he adds: "And this is the correct position."' Analyse the status of the transmitter and the opponent's options based on the passage.
?
Given a disputation where a Hanbali quotes a hadith without endorsing it, and the Shafi'i opponent objects to the hadith's authenticity. Determine if the objection is allowed according to the three criteria (self-evident, accepted, necessary).

What is Naql? Defining Transmission in Disputation
Author defines naql and introduces the transmitter's commitment as a key variable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define naql and identify when a transmitter becomes a claimant (muddaʿī) subject to the rules of disputation.

Core Idea
Naql is citing someone else's speech; committing to its truth turns you into a claimant.

In disputation, you often need to quote another scholar or authority. This act of quoting has specific rules that determine your role in the debate. This lesson introduces the definition of naql (transmission) and the pivotal distinction between a mere transmitter and a claimant who endorses the transmitted content.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition of naql
Find the author's definition: 'bringing someone else's speech while showing its attribution to the speaker.' Note that it covers any form: definition, division, assent, etc.
2
Examine the examples given
Look at the two examples: poetry by al-Mutanabbi and a Shafi'i fiqh ruling. They illustrate how naql can be from different fields (language and law) and still follow the same definition.
3
Recognize the two scenarios of transmitter commitment
The author distinguishes whether the transmitter commits to the truth of the transmitted content. If he does (e.g., saying 'this is the correct ruling'), he becomes a claimant (muddaʿī); if he does not, he is just a reporter.
The example of poetry commitment involves defending the expression's correctness.
4
Determine the resulting disputation type
When the transmitter is a claimant, the disputation follows the rules for assent (tasdiq). When he is not a claimant, the disputation concerns the correctness of the expression itself (if he committed to the expression) or only the transmission's authenticity (if no commitment).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٢)
المُنَاظَرَةُ فِي النَّقْلِ
النقل: هو إتيان الإنسان بكلام لغيره، مع إظهار إسناده إلى قائله، تعريفاً كان أو تقسيماً أو تصديقاً أو غير ذلك.
كقول الناقل: قال أبو الطيب المتنبي:
يا من يعزُّ علينا أن نفارقهم وجداننا كلَّ شيء بعدكم عدم
إن كان سرَّكُم ما قال حاسدنا فما لجرحٍ إذا أرضاكم ألمُ
وكقول الناقل: قال الشافعية:
لمس الرجل المرأة الأجنبية دون حائل ينقض الوضوء.
فإن التزم الناقل صحة مضمون الكلام الذي نقله، فقال مثلاً: وقول الشافعية هو الحكم الصحيح، فهو مدَّعٍ، ويُناظر على أساس أنه مدَّع ضمن ضوابط المناظرة في التصديق. أو قال مثلاً في قول أبي الطيب المتنبي:
هذي برزتِ لنا فهجتِ رسیـسا ثم انثَنَيْتِ وما شفيت نسيسا
وعبارة المتنبي هذه صحيحة لا اعتراض عليها، فهو بمثابة صاحب عبارة يناظرُ ضمن أصول المناظرة في العبارة.
أما إذا لم يلتزم صحة مضمون النقل: فإن كان النقل بديهياً، أو مسلماً به عند الخصم، أو معتبراً من ضروريات مذهبه، فليس (للسائل) أن يوجه إليه أي اعتراض. وإن كان النقل غير ذلك، كان للسائل أن يطالب بتصحيح النقل، أي: أن يطالب بإثبات صحة النقل وفق طرائق إثبات المرويات.
وقد يطلق في هذا الفن على المطالبة بتصحيح النقل عبارة: (منع الدعوى).
واختلفوا في طلب تصحيح النقل، فقيل: يجب، وقيل: يُستحسن، وقيل: إن كان (السائل) جاهلاً بصحة النقل، وجب طلب تصحيحه، وإن كان عالماً بأن النقل صحيح، لم يجز له طلب التصحيح.
(2) The Discussion of Transmission (al-naql): al-naql is a person's bringing of speech from someone else, while showing its attribution to its speaker, whether it be a definition, a division, an assent, or otherwise. Like the transmitter's saying: Abu al-Tayyib al-Mutanabbi said: 'O you whose separation is hard for us, we find everything after you is nonexistent. If what our envier said has pleased you, then there is no pain for a wound when it pleases you.' And like the transmitter's saying: The Shafi'is said: 'A man's touching a non-mahram woman without a barrier nullifies ablution.' If the transmitter commits to the soundness of the content of the speech he transmitted, saying for example: 'And the Shafi'is' statement is the correct ruling,' then he is a claimant (muddaʿī), and he is disputed on the basis that he is a claimant within the rules of disputation concerning assent. Or if he says, for example, regarding al-Mutanabbi's verse: 'This one appeared to us and stirred an ember, then turned away and did not cure the thirst,' and this phrase of al-Mutanabbi is correct with no objection to it, then he is like the owner of an expression who disputes within the principles of disputation concerning expression. As for when he does not commit to the soundness of the content of the transmission: if the transmission is self-evident, or accepted by the opponent, or considered among the necessities of his school, then it is not for the questioner (al-sāʾil) to direct any objection against it. But if the transmission is otherwise, then the questioner may demand correction of the transmission, i.e., demand proof of the soundness of the transmission according to the methods of verifying transmitted reports. And in this art, the demand for correction of the transmission is sometimes expressed as 'prevention of the claim' (manʿ al-daʿwā). They differed regarding demanding correction of the transmission: some said it is obligatory, some said it is commendable, and some said that if the questioner is ignorant of the soundness of the transmission, it is obligatory to demand its correction, but if he knows that the transmission is sound, he is not permitted to demand correction.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is a manual for disputation (munazara) concerning the concept of 'transmission' (naql)—citing others' statements. The author begins by defining naql as bringing someone else's speech while attributing it to the original speaker, whether that speech is a definition, division, assent, etc. He then provides examples: one from al-Mutanabbi's poetry and one from the Shafi'i school's ruling on wudū' nullification. The core of the passage differentiates two scenarios based on the transmitter's commitment. If the transmitter commits to the truth of the transmitted content (e.g., endorsing the Shafi'i ruling as correct), he becomes a claimant (muddaʿī) and is subject to the rules of disputation concerning assent. If he commits to the correctness of a poetic expression, he is treated as the author of that expression in disputation. If the transmitter does not commit to the truth of the content, then the opponent (sāʾil) may object only if the transmission is not self-evident, not accepted by the opponent, and not a necessity of the opponent's school. Otherwise, the opponent cannot object. If objection is allowed, the opponent can demand proof of the transmission's authenticity (taṣḥīḥ al-naql). The author notes that this demand is sometimes called 'prevention of the claim' (manʿ al-daʿwā). Finally, there is a discussion on the rule for demanding correction: whether it is obligatory, commendable, or conditional on the opponent's knowledge—if the opponent knows the transmission is sound, he may not demand correction. The examples are all from Islamic sciences (poetry and fiqh).

Key Terms
النقل
السائل
المدَّعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens when a transmitter says: 'This is the correct ruling' after quoting the Shafiʿi position?
A) He is a mere transmitter and no disputation rules apply.
B) He becomes a claimant (muddaʿī) and disputation follows rules for assent.
C) He becomes the author of the statement and disputation follows rules for expression.
D) He is considered to have committed only to the attribution, not the truth.

Key Takeaway
The definition of naql hinges on attribution, and the transmitter's commitment to truth transforms him from a reporter to a claimant with a burden of proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Read the following transmission: 'Imam Ahmad said: “Every intoxicant is haram.”' Then the speaker adds: 'And this is the correct position.' Determine: (a) What is the naql? (b) Is the speaker a claimant (muddaʿī)? (c) What type of disputation rules apply?
?
Task 2: In a debate about a poetic line, the transmitter says: 'This line is mine.' Is he a claimant? What rules apply according to the author?

When Can You Challenge a Transmission? Rules for Demanding Proof
Author outlines conditions for challenging transmission and discusses obligation of taṣḥīḥ al-naql.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when a questioner (sāʾil) may demand taṣḥīḥ al-naql and know the differing opinions on the obligation to do so.

Core Idea
Challenge a transmission only if it's not self-evident, not accepted, and not a school necessity.

After understanding the definition of naql, the author provides the rules for when the opponent (sāʾil) can object to a transmission. Not every transmission is open to challenge; some are immune. This lesson explains those conditions and the debate about whether demanding proof is obligatory, commendable, or conditional on the questioner's knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify when objection is forbidden
The author lists three cases where the questioner cannot object: if the transmission is self-evident (badīhī), if it is accepted by the opponent (musallam bihi ʿinda al-khaṣm), or if it is considered a necessity of the opponent's school (ḍarūriyyāt madhhabihi).
2
Determine when objection is allowed
If the transmission falls outside these three categories, the questioner may demand correction of the transmission (taṣḥīḥ al-naql), i.e., proof of its authenticity.
This demand is sometimes called 'manʿ al-daʿwā' (prevention of the claim).
3
Understand the differing opinions on demanding correction
The author reports three positions: some say it is obligatory (wājib), some say it is commendable (yustaḥsanu), and some say it is obligatory only if the questioner is ignorant of the transmission's authenticity; if he knows it is sound, he may not demand correction.
4
Apply the conditions to a scenario
Consider a transmitted hadith that is well-known to both parties. According to the rule, can the questioner object? No, because it is either self-evident, accepted, or a school necessity. The questioner must first determine the status of the transmission.

Source Text

According to the passage, which of the following transmissions CAN the questioner object to?
A) A transmission that is self-evident (badīhī).
B) A transmission accepted by the opponent (musallam bihi).
